--- Lesson 7.1:  (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Cell Structure and Function
Key Concepts: Cell Structure and Function

This lesson covers the fundamental concepts of cell structure and function in biology.

  • Understanding the basic principles and terminology
  • Exploring real-world applications and examples
  • Connecting to other biological concepts

Learning Objectives

  • Define key terms related to cell structure and function
  • Explain the importance of this topic in biology
  • Apply concepts to solve problems

Why This Matters

  • Builds foundation for advanced biology topics
  • Has practical applications in medicine and research
  • Helps explain natural phenomena

--- Lesson 7.1:  (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What are the three tenets of cell theory, and who were the key scientists who developed it?
    [ ] Cell theory states that only animals are made of cells, cells cannot reproduce, and cells arise spontaneously — it was developed by Charles Darwin in 1859
    [*] Cell theory states: (1) all living things are made of one or more cells, (2) the cell is the basic unit of life, and (3) all new cells arise from pre-existing cells — developed by Matthias Schleiden (plants, 1838), Theodor Schwann (animals, 1839), and Rudolf Virchow ('omnis cellula e cellula,' 1855), building on Robert Hooke's first observation of cells in cork (1665) and Antonie van Leeuwenhoek's observation of living cells (1670s)
    [ ] Cell theory states that cells are the smallest particles of matter, that cells are found only in plants, and that cells are created from non-living chemicals — proposed by Robert Brown in 1831
    [ ] Cell theory states that cells are made of atoms, that cells exist only in multicellular organisms, and that cells form from crystallization — developed by Louis Pasteur
    Explanation: Cell theory — foundation of modern biology: (1) Three tenets: all living things are composed of one or more cells; the cell is the fundamental unit of structure and function in living organisms; all cells arise from pre-existing cells by division. (2) Historical development: Robert Hooke (1665) coined 'cell' while examining cork under a microscope (Micrographia) — saw empty cell walls. Antonie van Leeuwenhoek (1670s) — first to observe living cells ('animalcules' — bacteria, protists, sperm cells) using single-lens microscopes with ~270× magnification. Matthias Schleiden (1838) — concluded all plant tissues are composed of cells. Theodor Schwann (1839) — extended to animal tissues. Rudolf Virchow (1855) — 'omnis cellula e cellula' (every cell from a cell) — refuted spontaneous generation of cells. (3) Modern additions: cells contain hereditary information (DNA) passed to daughter cells; all cells have the same basic chemical composition; energy flow occurs within cells. (4) Cell sizes: most 1-100 μm. Smallest: Mycoplasma (~0.2 μm). Largest: ostrich egg (~170 mm), Caulerpa taxifolia (single-celled alga, up to 3 m). Human cells: ~37.2 trillion.

  Q2. What are the key differences between prokaryotic and eukaryotic cells?
    [ ] Prokaryotic cells are larger and more complex than eukaryotic cells — they have a nucleus, mitochondria, and extensive internal membranes, while eukaryotic cells lack these features
    [*] Prokaryotic cells (bacteria and archaea) lack a membrane-bound nucleus (DNA is in a nucleoid region) and lack membrane-bound organelles — they are typically smaller (0.2-5 μm), have 70S ribosomes, and may have a cell wall (peptidoglycan in bacteria) — eukaryotic cells (protists, fungi, plants, animals) have a true nucleus enclosed by a nuclear envelope, membrane-bound organelles (mitochondria, ER, Golgi), 80S ribosomes, and are typically larger (10-100 μm)
    [ ] There are no significant differences — prokaryotic and eukaryotic cells have the same organelles and structures but differ only in the organisms they are found in
    [ ] Prokaryotic cells are exclusively multicellular organisms while eukaryotic cells are always single-celled — this is the only distinction between the two types
    Explanation: Prokaryotic vs. eukaryotic cells: (1) Prokaryotes ('before nucleus'): Domains Bacteria and Archaea. No nuclear envelope — circular DNA in nucleoid region (+ plasmids). No membrane-bound organelles. Small: 0.2-5 μm typically. 70S ribosomes (50S + 30S subunits). Cell wall: peptidoglycan in bacteria (Gram + thick, Gram − thin + outer membrane); pseudopeptidoglycan or S-layer in archaea. Reproduce by binary fission. (2) Eukaryotes ('true nucleus'): Domain Eukarya — protists, fungi, plants, animals. True nucleus with double membrane (nuclear envelope with nuclear pores). Membrane-bound organelles: mitochondria, ER, Golgi, lysosomes, (chloroplasts in plants/algae). Larger: 10-100 μm typically. 80S ribosomes (60S + 40S). Linear chromosomes with histones. Reproduce by mitosis/meiosis. (3) Endosymbiotic theory (Lynn Margulis, 1967): mitochondria and chloroplasts were once free-living prokaryotes engulfed by ancestral eukaryotic cells — evidence: double membranes, own circular DNA, 70S ribosomes, binary fission-like division, size similar to bacteria.

  Q3. What is the structure of the cell membrane according to the fluid mosaic model?
    [ ] The cell membrane is a rigid, solid wall of pure protein that forms an impermeable barrier around the cell, preventing all substances from entering or leaving
    [*] According to the fluid mosaic model (Singer & Nicolson, 1972), the cell membrane is a phospholipid bilayer with hydrophilic heads facing outward and hydrophobic tails facing inward — proteins are embedded in or attached to this bilayer (integral/transmembrane proteins and peripheral proteins), along with cholesterol (in animal cells, regulates fluidity), glycoproteins, and glycolipids — the membrane is 'fluid' because components can move laterally
    [ ] The cell membrane is a single layer of carbohydrate molecules with no lipids or proteins, functioning only as a physical shield against mechanical damage
    [ ] The cell membrane is composed entirely of DNA wrapped around histone proteins, serving both as a boundary and as the cell's genetic material storage site
    Explanation: Cell membrane — fluid mosaic model: (1) Phospholipid bilayer: each phospholipid has a hydrophilic head (phosphate group + glycerol) and two hydrophobic fatty acid tails → spontaneously forms bilayer in aqueous environment. ~5 nm thick. (2) 'Fluid': lipids and proteins move laterally within the bilayer (lateral diffusion: ~2 μm/sec for lipids). Flip-flop (transverse movement) is rare without flippase enzymes. (3) 'Mosaic': diverse proteins scattered through the membrane. Integral/transmembrane proteins: span entire bilayer (channels, carriers, receptors). Peripheral proteins: attached to surface (enzymes, cytoskeletal anchors). (4) Cholesterol: ~20% of animal cell membrane lipids. Wedges between phospholipids — at high temperature reduces fluidity (restricts movement); at low temperature prevents solidification (disrupts packing). Absent in most prokaryotes. (5) Glycocalyx: carbohydrate chains on glycoproteins and glycolipids (extracellular surface) — cell recognition, protection, cell-cell adhesion. Blood type antigens (A, B, O) are glycolipids/glycoproteins. (6) Selective permeability: small nonpolar molecules (O₂, CO₂, N₂) pass freely; small polar molecules (H₂O, ethanol) pass slowly; ions and large molecules require transport proteins.

  Q4. What is the structure and function of the nucleus in eukaryotic cells?
    [ ] The nucleus is a small, membrane-free region that contains only RNA — it functions exclusively to produce lipids for the cell membrane and has no role in genetic information
    [*] The nucleus is surrounded by a double membrane (nuclear envelope) with nuclear pores that regulate transport — it contains the cell's DNA organized into chromatin (dispersed during interphase) or chromosomes (condensed during division), a nucleolus (site of ribosomal RNA synthesis and ribosome assembly), and serves as the control center directing gene expression, DNA replication, and cell division
    [ ] The nucleus is the site of ATP production through oxidative phosphorylation — it contains cristae and an electron transport chain identical to mitochondria
    [ ] The nucleus is found only in plant cells and functions exclusively to store starch granules and pigment molecules used in photosynthesis
    Explanation: The nucleus — command center of the cell: (1) Nuclear envelope: double membrane (outer continuous with rough ER). Nuclear pores (~3,000-4,000 per nucleus): large protein complexes (nucleoporins, ~120 MDa) that regulate bidirectional transport — small molecules diffuse freely; large molecules (proteins, RNA) require nuclear localization signals (NLS) and importin/exportin proteins (Ran-GTP cycle). (2) Chromatin: DNA + histone proteins. Euchromatin (loosely packed, transcriptionally active) vs. heterochromatin (tightly packed, mostly inactive). During cell division: chromatin condenses into visible chromosomes. Humans: 46 chromosomes (23 pairs). (3) Nucleolus: dense region within nucleus (not membrane-bound). Site of rRNA transcription (by RNA Pol I) and ribosome subunit assembly. rRNA genes are clustered on chromosomes 13, 14, 15, 21, 22 in humans (nucleolar organizer regions). Can have 1-4 nucleoli per nucleus. (4) Nuclear lamina: meshwork of intermediate filaments (lamins A, B, C) lining inner nuclear membrane — provides structural support, anchors chromatin, involved in DNA replication. Mutations in lamin A → progeria (Hutchinson-Gilford syndrome: premature aging). (5) Functions: DNA storage/protection, transcription (DNA → mRNA), RNA processing (splicing, capping, polyadenylation), ribosome assembly, cell cycle regulation.

  Q5. How do mitochondria produce ATP, and what evidence supports the endosymbiotic theory of their origin?
    [ ] Mitochondria produce ATP by photosynthesis using chlorophyll pigments — they originated from plant cells that were incorporated into animal cells through grafting
    [*] Mitochondria produce ATP through cellular respiration: pyruvate from glycolysis enters the matrix for the citric acid cycle (generating NADH and FADH₂), then electrons pass through the electron transport chain (complexes I-IV on the inner membrane/cristae), creating a proton gradient that drives ATP synthase (chemiosmosis) — producing ~30-32 ATP per glucose — endosymbiotic evidence includes their own circular DNA, 70S ribosomes, double membrane, binary fission-like division, and size similar to bacteria
    [ ] Mitochondria produce ATP by breaking down DNA into nucleotides — they have always been part of eukaryotic cells and share no evolutionary relationship with bacteria
    [ ] Mitochondria do not produce ATP — they function only as storage vesicles for calcium ions and were formed by invagination of the cell membrane during evolution
    Explanation: Mitochondria — powerhouse of the cell: (1) Structure: double membrane — outer membrane (permeable via porins), inner membrane (highly folded into cristae — greatly increases surface area for ATP production), intermembrane space, matrix (contains enzymes, circular DNA, 70S ribosomes). (2) ATP production pathway: glycolysis (cytoplasm): glucose → 2 pyruvate + 2 ATP + 2 NADH. Pyruvate enters mitochondrial matrix → pyruvate dehydrogenase → acetyl-CoA + CO₂ + NADH. Citric acid (Krebs) cycle: acetyl-CoA → 2 CO₂ + 3 NADH + 1 FADH₂ + 1 GTP per turn (×2 per glucose). Electron transport chain (inner membrane): NADH → Complex I → ubiquinone → Complex III → cytochrome c → Complex IV → O₂ + H₂O. FADH₂ → Complex II → ubiquinone. Protons pumped to intermembrane space → proton-motive force. ATP synthase (Complex V): H⁺ flow back through rotary motor → ADP + Pi → ATP. (3) Yield: ~30-32 ATP per glucose (variable). (4) Endosymbiotic theory (Lynn Margulis, 1967): ancestral eukaryote engulfed aerobic α-proteobacterium → mutualistic endosymbiosis. Evidence: own circular DNA (~16,569 bp in humans, encoding 37 genes); 70S ribosomes (bacterial type); double membrane (inner = original bacterial membrane); divide by binary fission independently of cell cycle; size/shape similar to bacteria (~1-10 μm); phylogenetic analysis shows mitochondrial genes are closest to Rickettsiales (α-proteobacteria). (5) Maternal inheritance: mitochondrial DNA inherited exclusively from mother (egg contributes ~100,000 mitochondria; sperm mitochondria are destroyed after fertilization).

  Q6. What are ribosomes, and how do free and bound ribosomes differ in function?
    [ ] Ribosomes are membrane-bound organelles that produce lipids — free ribosomes make phospholipids while bound ribosomes make cholesterol, and they are found only in eukaryotic cells
    [*] Ribosomes are complexes of rRNA and protein that synthesize proteins by translating mRNA — free ribosomes (in cytoplasm) produce proteins used within the cell (cytoplasmic enzymes, cytoskeletal proteins), while bound ribosomes (attached to rough ER) produce proteins destined for secretion, membrane insertion, or organelle targeting — eukaryotic ribosomes are 80S (60S + 40S) while prokaryotic are 70S (50S + 30S)
    [ ] Ribosomes are DNA-replicating structures found only in the nucleus — there is no difference between free and bound ribosomes as they both replicate DNA at the same rate
    [ ] Ribosomes are storage granules that accumulate starch and glycogen — free ribosomes store starch while bound ribosomes store glycogen in specialized vacuoles
    Explanation: Ribosomes — protein synthesis machinery: (1) Structure: not membrane-bound organelles — composed of ribosomal RNA (rRNA) + proteins. Two subunits: large + small. Eukaryotic: 80S total (60S large + 40S small; 'S' = Svedberg unit, sedimentation rate — not additive). Prokaryotic: 70S (50S + 30S). (2) Function: translate mRNA into polypeptide chains. mRNA binds to small subunit → initiator tRNA (Met-tRNA) binds to start codon (AUG) → large subunit joins → elongation: aminoacyl-tRNAs enter A site, peptide bond forms (peptidyl transferase — actually a ribozyme, catalyzed by rRNA), translocation to P site → termination at stop codon (UAA, UAG, UGA). (3) Free ribosomes: float in cytoplasm. Produce proteins for intracellular use — cytoplasmic enzymes (glycolytic enzymes), cytoskeletal proteins (actin, tubulin), peripheral membrane proteins. (4) Bound ribosomes: attached to rough ER membrane. Produce proteins with signal peptides (N-terminal hydrophobic sequence recognized by signal recognition particle/SRP) → protein threaded into ER lumen → folding, glycosylation → vesicle transport to Golgi. Products: secreted proteins (hormones, antibodies), membrane proteins (receptors, channels), lysosomal enzymes. (5) Same ribosomes can be free or bound — determined by the mRNA being translated (presence/absence of signal peptide sequence). (6) Clinical significance: prokaryotic vs. eukaryotic ribosome differences are exploited by antibiotics — tetracycline (blocks tRNA binding to 30S), chloramphenicol (inhibits peptidyl transferase on 50S), erythromycin (blocks translocation on 50S). These don't affect human 80S ribosomes → selective toxicity.

  Q7. What are the structural and functional differences between rough ER and smooth ER?
    [*] Rough ER has ribosomes on its surface and is involved in protein synthesis, folding, and modification — smooth ER lacks ribosomes and is involved in lipid synthesis, calcium storage, and detoxification — both are continuous membrane systems connected to the nuclear envelope
    [ ] Rough ER and smooth ER are identical in structure and function — the 'rough' designation simply refers to ER that has been damaged during cell aging
    [ ] Rough ER produces ATP while smooth ER produces DNA — they are separate, unconnected organelles found in different cell types
    [ ] Rough ER is found only in plant cells and smooth ER is found only in animal cells — neither is involved in protein or lipid synthesis
    Explanation: Endoplasmic reticulum (ER): (1) Rough ER (RER): studded with ribosomes on cytoplasmic surface. Functions: protein synthesis (secretory, membrane, lysosomal proteins translated on bound ribosomes), protein folding (chaperones: BiP/GRP78, calnexin), quality control (misfolded proteins → ERAD: ER-associated degradation → proteasome), N-linked glycosylation (oligosaccharide transferred to Asn residues by oligosaccharyltransferase), disulfide bond formation (protein disulfide isomerase/PDI). Prominent in cells that secrete proteins: plasma cells (antibodies), pancreatic acinar cells (digestive enzymes), goblet cells (mucus). (2) Smooth ER (SER): no ribosomes. Functions: lipid/phospholipid synthesis (phosphatidylcholine, sphingomyelin), steroid hormone synthesis (in adrenal cortex, gonads — SER abundant), detoxification (cytochrome P450 enzymes in liver — oxidize drugs, toxins, ethanol — chronic alcohol → SER proliferation), carbohydrate metabolism (glucose-6-phosphatase in liver converts G6P → glucose for export), calcium storage/release (sarcoplasmic reticulum in muscle — specialized SER that releases Ca²⁺ for contraction). (3) ER stress: accumulation of misfolded proteins → unfolded protein response (UPR) → if unresolved → apoptosis. Relevant in: diabetes (β-cells), neurodegenerative diseases (Alzheimer's, Parkinson's).

  Q8. What is the function of the Golgi apparatus, and how does it process and sort proteins?
    [ ] The Golgi apparatus is the cell's energy-producing organelle that generates ATP by oxidizing fatty acids — it has no role in protein modification or sorting
    [*] The Golgi apparatus (discovered by Camillo Golgi, 1898) receives proteins from the ER in transport vesicles, then modifies them (glycosylation, phosphorylation, sulfation), sorts them by adding molecular 'address tags' (e.g., mannose-6-phosphate tag directs proteins to lysosomes), and packages them into vesicles for delivery to their final destinations — the plasma membrane, lysosomes, or secretion outside the cell
    [ ] The Golgi apparatus stores DNA and produces ribosomes — it is identical in function to the nucleolus and is found only in prokaryotic cells
    [ ] The Golgi apparatus is a temporary structure that forms only during cell division to separate chromosomes — it disassembles after mitosis is complete
    Explanation: Golgi apparatus — the cell's processing and shipping center: (1) Structure: stack of flattened membrane-bound sacs (cisternae), typically 4-8 per stack. Polarized: cis face (receiving side, near ER) → medial cisternae → trans face (shipping side, near plasma membrane). Trans-Golgi network (TGN): sorting station. (2) Receiving: COPII-coated vesicles bud from ER → fuse with cis-Golgi. (3) Modification: further glycosylation (N-linked oligosaccharides trimmed and modified; O-linked glycosylation added); phosphorylation; sulfation; proteolytic cleavage (e.g., proinsulin → insulin + C-peptide). (4) Sorting: mannose-6-phosphate (M6P) tag → M6P receptor → lysosomal delivery. Signal sequences on proteins determine routing. (5) Packaging and export: constitutive secretion (default pathway — continuous vesicle fusion with plasma membrane), regulated secretion (vesicles stored, released on signal — e.g., neurotransmitters, hormones), retrograde transport (COPI vesicles: Golgi → ER, retrieves escaped ER-resident proteins with KDEL signal). (6) Camillo Golgi discovered the apparatus in 1898 using silver nitrate staining of Purkinje neurons — shared Nobel Prize 1906 with Santiago Ramón y Cajal. (7) Clinical: I-cell disease (mucolipidosis II) — deficiency of GlcNAc phosphotransferase → proteins lack M6P tag → lysosomal enzymes secreted instead of delivered to lysosomes → cellular waste accumulates.

  Q9. What are lysosomes, and what happens when lysosomal function is impaired?
    [ ] Lysosomes are organelles that produce new proteins by translating mRNA — when impaired, the cell simply produces proteins more slowly but suffers no other consequences
    [*] Lysosomes are membrane-bound organelles containing ~50 types of hydrolytic (digestive) enzymes that function at acidic pH (~4.5-5.0, maintained by H⁺-ATPase proton pumps) — they break down worn-out organelles (autophagy), engulfed pathogens (phagocytosis), and macromolecules — when lysosomal enzymes are deficient, undigested material accumulates, causing lysosomal storage diseases (e.g., Tay-Sachs: missing hexosaminidase A → ganglioside accumulation → neurodegeneration; Gaucher disease: missing glucocerebrosidase)
    [ ] Lysosomes are structural scaffolds that support the cell membrane from the inside — when impaired, cells simply become floppier but continue to function normally without any disease
    [ ] Lysosomes are the site of DNA replication in the cell — when impaired, cells cannot divide but all other cellular functions continue normally
    Explanation: Lysosomes — cellular recycling center: (1) Discovery: Christian de Duve (Nobel Prize 1974) — discovered lysosomes using cell fractionation and centrifugation. (2) Structure: single membrane. Interior maintained at pH 4.5-5.0 by vacuolar H⁺-ATPase (proton pumps). ~50+ types of acid hydrolases (active only at acidic pH — safety mechanism: if lysosome ruptures, enzymes are inactive at cytoplasmic pH ~7.2). (3) Functions: autophagy (recycling worn-out organelles — mitochondria every ~10 days → mitophagy), phagocytosis (immune cells digest engulfed pathogens: phagosome + lysosome → phagolysosome), endocytosis (break down materials brought into cell), apoptosis (programmed cell death when lysosomal membrane permeabilizes → cathepsins released). (4) Lysosomal storage diseases (~50 known): genetic deficiency of specific lysosomal enzyme → substrate accumulates. Tay-Sachs: hexosaminidase A deficiency → GM2 ganglioside accumulates in neurons → neurodegeneration, death by age 4-5 (carrier frequency 1/30 in Ashkenazi Jewish population). Gaucher disease: glucocerebrosidase deficiency → glucocerebroside accumulates in macrophages → enlarged spleen/liver (most common lysosomal storage disease; treatable with enzyme replacement therapy — imiglucerase). Pompe disease: acid maltase deficiency → glycogen accumulates → muscle weakness. (5) Autophagy research: Yoshinori Ohsumi, Nobel Prize 2016 — elucidated mechanisms of autophagy in yeast.

  Q10. What are the three main components of the cytoskeleton, and what functions does each perform?
    [ ] The cytoskeleton consists of only one type of protein fiber (collagen) that provides rigidity — it has no role in cell movement or intracellular transport
    [*] The three main components are: (1) microfilaments (actin, ~7 nm diameter) — involved in cell movement, muscle contraction, cytokinesis, and cell shape; (2) microtubules (tubulin, ~25 nm diameter) — form the mitotic spindle, cilia/flagella, and intracellular transport tracks for motor proteins (kinesin and dynein); (3) intermediate filaments (~10 nm, e.g., keratin, vimentin, neurofilaments) — provide mechanical strength and resist tensile forces
    [ ] The cytoskeleton is made of carbohydrate chains, lipid tubules, and nucleic acid filaments — it functions only during cell division and disassembles between divisions
    [ ] The cytoskeleton exists only in prokaryotic cells and consists of peptidoglycan, flagellin, and chitin — eukaryotic cells rely entirely on the cell wall for structural support
    Explanation: Cytoskeleton — the cell's internal framework: (1) Microfilaments (actin filaments): thinnest (~7 nm). Composed of G-actin monomers polymerized into F-actin (double helix). Functions: cell shape (cortical actin network beneath membrane), cell movement (lamellipodia, filopodia — amoeboid movement), muscle contraction (actin + myosin sliding filament model), cytokinesis (contractile ring pinches cell in two), microvilli (intestinal epithelium — increase surface area 20×). Dynamic: constant polymerization/depolymerization (treadmilling). Drugs: cytochalasin B (blocks polymerization), phalloidin (stabilizes filaments). (2) Microtubules: largest (~25 nm). Composed of α-tubulin and β-tubulin heterodimers forming hollow tubes. Functions: mitotic spindle (separates chromosomes during cell division), intracellular transport (motor proteins: kinesin = + end/anterograde; dynein = − end/retrograde), cell shape, cilia (9+2 arrangement — respiratory epithelium, fallopian tubes) and flagella (9+2 — sperm), centrioles (9×3 arrangement). Emanate from centrosome/MTOC. Drugs: colchicine (blocks polymerization — used for gout), taxol/paclitaxel (stabilizes — anticancer drug, prevents mitotic spindle disassembly), vincristine/vinblastine (block polymerization — anticancer). (3) Intermediate filaments: medium (~10 nm). Most stable/least dynamic. Types: keratins (epithelial — hair, nails, skin), vimentin (mesenchymal — connective tissue), neurofilaments (neurons — axon caliber), lamins (nuclear lamina — structural support for nucleus), desmin (muscle). Functions: mechanical strength, resist shearing forces, anchor organelles. Not found in many single-celled eukaryotes.

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes cell theory?
    [ ] A hypothesis stating that only multicellular organisms contain cells and that single-celled organisms are not considered living
    [*] A foundational biological principle stating that (1) all living things are made of one or more cells, (2) the cell is the basic unit of structure and function in living organisms, and (3) all new cells arise from pre-existing cells through division — developed by Schleiden (1838), Schwann (1839), and Virchow (1855), building on Hooke's first observation of cells (1665)
    [ ] A theory that cells form spontaneously from non-living matter whenever the right chemical conditions exist
    [ ] A classification system that divides cells into four kingdoms based on their metabolic rate and reproductive strategy
    Explanation: Cell theory — three tenets and historical context: (1) First tenet: all living organisms are composed of one or more cells (unicellular: bacteria, protists, some fungi; multicellular: plants, animals, most fungi). Viruses are not cells and are debated as 'living.' (2) Second tenet: the cell is the fundamental unit of structure (smallest unit that displays all characteristics of life) and function in all living organisms. (3) Third tenet: all cells arise from pre-existing cells by division — Virchow's 'omnis cellula e cellula' (1855) definitively refuted spontaneous generation for cells. (4) Modern additions: cells contain hereditary information (DNA/RNA) that is passed during division; all cells have the same basic chemical composition (water, organic molecules, ions); energy flow (metabolism) occurs within cells. (5) Exceptions/special cases: striated muscle fibers (syncytia — multinucleated), red blood cells (anucleate in mammals — lose nucleus during maturation), viruses (not cells, but can reproduce using host cells). (6) Importance: unifying principle that connects all of biology — from molecular biology to ecology.

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes the nucleus?
    [ ] A small, membrane-free organelle found in the cytoplasm that produces ATP through oxidative phosphorylation
    [*] A large, double-membrane-bound organelle that serves as the cell's control center — containing the cell's DNA organized into chromatin/chromosomes, a nucleolus (for rRNA synthesis and ribosome assembly), and nuclear pores that regulate transport of mRNA, proteins, and ribosomal subunits between the nucleus and cytoplasm — directing gene expression, DNA replication, and cell division
    [ ] A digestive compartment containing hydrolytic enzymes at acidic pH that breaks down cellular waste and engulfed pathogens
    [ ] A network of protein fibers including actin and tubulin that provides structural support and enables cell movement
    Explanation: Nucleus — the cell's command center: (1) Structure: largest organelle (~5-10 μm diameter in animal cells, ~10% of cell volume). Double membrane (nuclear envelope) — outer membrane continuous with rough ER. Nuclear pores: ~3,000-4,000 complexes per nucleus, each ~120 MDa (composed of ~30 different nucleoporins), diameter ~9 nm (passive) or ~40 nm (active transport with NLS). Nuclear lamina: meshwork of lamin proteins (intermediate filaments) along inner membrane — structural support + chromatin anchoring. (2) Contents: chromatin (DNA + histones) — euchromatin (active, loosely packed) and heterochromatin (inactive, condensed). Nucleolus: 1-4 per nucleus, site of rRNA transcription (RNA Pol I → 45S pre-rRNA → 28S, 18S, 5.8S rRNA) and ribosome subunit assembly. (3) Functions: DNA replication during S phase; transcription (DNA → pre-mRNA by RNA Pol II); RNA processing (5' capping, splicing, 3' polyadenylation); ribosome assembly; cell cycle control. (4) Clinical relevance: laminopathies (mutations in lamin genes → Hutchinson-Gilford progeria, Emery-Dreifuss muscular dystrophy, dilated cardiomyopathy). Nuclear morphology changes in cancer cells (enlarged nuclei, irregular shape, prominent nucleoli — used in histological diagnosis).

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes cytoplasm?
    [ ] The rigid outer covering of plant cells composed of cellulose microfibrils that provides structural support and protection
    [*] The gel-like material (cytosol) that fills the interior of the cell between the cell membrane and the nucleus — consisting of water (~70-80%), dissolved ions, small molecules, and macromolecules — plus all the organelles (mitochondria, ER, Golgi, ribosomes, cytoskeleton) suspended within it — serving as the site for many metabolic reactions including glycolysis, protein synthesis on free ribosomes, and signal transduction pathways
    [ ] A specialized membrane transport protein that actively pumps sodium and potassium ions across the cell membrane
    [ ] The extracellular fluid surrounding cells in multicellular organisms that delivers nutrients and removes waste products
    Explanation: Cytoplasm — the cellular interior: (1) Definition: everything between the cell membrane and the nuclear envelope (in eukaryotes) or everything within the cell membrane (in prokaryotes). Includes cytosol + organelles + inclusions. (2) Cytosol: the liquid portion (~70-80% water). Contains dissolved ions (K⁺, Na⁺, Cl⁻, Ca²⁺, Mg²⁺), small molecules (amino acids, nucleotides, sugars, metabolite intermediates), and macromolecules (proteins at ~200-300 mg/mL — extremely crowded environment → molecular crowding effects). pH ~7.2. Reducing environment (high glutathione concentration). (3) Functions occurring in cytosol: glycolysis (glucose → pyruvate), gluconeogenesis, fatty acid synthesis, amino acid biosynthesis, pentose phosphate pathway, protein synthesis (free ribosomes), signal transduction cascades, proteasomal protein degradation (ubiquitin-proteasome system). (4) Organelles within cytoplasm: mitochondria, ER (rough and smooth), Golgi apparatus, lysosomes, peroxisomes, ribosomes, cytoskeleton. (5) Inclusions: non-membrane-bound structures — glycogen granules (liver, muscle), lipid droplets (adipocytes), melanin granules (melanocytes), crystal inclusions. (6) Cytoplasmic streaming (cyclosis): movement of cytoplasm within cells — driven by actin-myosin interactions. Especially important in large plant cells (Elodea, Chara) for distributing nutrients and organelles.

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes mitochondria?
    [ ] Single-membrane organelles found specifically in plant cells that capture light energy and convert it to chemical energy through photosynthesis
    [*] Double-membrane-bound organelles known as the 'powerhouse of the cell' that produce ATP through cellular respiration — featuring an outer membrane, inner membrane folded into cristae (increasing surface area for the electron transport chain and ATP synthase), a matrix containing enzymes for the citric acid cycle, and their own circular DNA and 70S ribosomes (evidence for endosymbiotic origin from ancient α-proteobacteria)
    [ ] Stacks of flattened membrane sacs that modify, sort, and package proteins for transport to various cellular destinations
    [ ] The portion of the cytoskeleton composed of hollow tubulin polymers that form the mitotic spindle during cell division
    Explanation: Mitochondria — powerhouse of the cell: (1) Structure: ~1-10 μm length, ~0.5-1 μm diameter (size of a bacterium). Double membrane: outer membrane (contains porins — permeable to small molecules <5 kDa); inner membrane (highly folded into cristae — contains ETC complexes I-IV + ATP synthase + carrier proteins; impermeable to most molecules — requires specific transporters). Intermembrane space: ~pH 7.0, accumulates H⁺ during oxidative phosphorylation. Matrix: contains citric acid cycle enzymes, mtDNA, 70S ribosomes, Ca²⁺ stores. (2) Number per cell: varies by energy demand — liver cell: ~2,000; red blood cell: 0 (mature); muscle cell: up to 10,000; oocyte: ~100,000. (3) Energy production: overall glucose → 30-32 ATP. Pyruvate (from cytoplasmic glycolysis) → matrix → acetyl-CoA → citric acid cycle → NADH + FADH₂ → ETC → proton gradient → ATP synthase. (4) Other functions: calcium signaling/storage, apoptosis (cytochrome c release → caspase activation), iron-sulfur cluster assembly, heme synthesis, fatty acid β-oxidation. (5) Endosymbiotic theory: circular mtDNA (16,569 bp in humans, 37 genes: 13 proteins + 22 tRNAs + 2 rRNAs), 70S ribosomes, double membrane, binary fission, cardiolipin in inner membrane (also in bacterial membranes), phylogenetically related to α-proteobacteria (Rickettsiales). (6) Clinical: mitochondrial diseases (maternal inheritance) — MELAS, Leber hereditary optic neuropathy; aging (mitochondrial theory of aging — accumulated mtDNA mutations); cancer (Warburg effect — cancer cells prefer glycolysis even with O₂).

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes a ribosome?
    [ ] A membrane-bound vesicle containing digestive enzymes that breaks down cellular waste at an acidic pH maintained by proton pumps
    [*] A non-membrane-bound cellular structure composed of ribosomal RNA (rRNA) and proteins that serves as the site of protein synthesis — translating mRNA codons into amino acid sequences — found as 80S particles in eukaryotes (60S + 40S subunits) and 70S in prokaryotes (50S + 30S) — existing as free ribosomes in the cytoplasm (producing intracellular proteins) or bound to the rough ER (producing secretory and membrane proteins)
    [ ] A long, hollow protein tube made of tubulin subunits that forms the structural framework for cilia and flagella
    [ ] A double-membrane organelle that contains its own DNA and produces energy through chemiosmosis and the electron transport chain
    Explanation: Ribosome — the protein factory: (1) Structure: composed of rRNA (~60% by weight) + ribosomal proteins (~40%). Not membrane-bound. Two subunits that join during translation. Eukaryotic: 80S (60S large: 28S + 5.8S + 5S rRNA + ~49 proteins; 40S small: 18S rRNA + ~33 proteins). Prokaryotic: 70S (50S large: 23S + 5S rRNA + ~34 proteins; 30S small: 16S rRNA + ~21 proteins). Mitochondrial and chloroplast ribosomes: 55S and 70S respectively. (2) Function: translation of mRNA → polypeptide. Three binding sites: A (aminoacyl — incoming charged tRNA), P (peptidyl — growing polypeptide chain), E (exit — discharged tRNA leaves). Peptidyl transferase activity: catalyzed by 23S/28S rRNA (ribozyme — RNA catalysis, not protein). Translation rate: ~15-20 amino acids/second in bacteria; ~5-6 in eukaryotes. Polyribosomes (polysomes): multiple ribosomes translating same mRNA simultaneously. (3) Free vs. bound: determined by signal peptide on nascent protein. Signal recognition particle (SRP) binds signal peptide → docks ribosome on ER → translation continues into ER lumen. (4) Clinical: antibiotics target prokaryotic (70S) ribosomes selectively — tetracycline (blocks 30S A-site), aminoglycosides (cause 30S misreading), chloramphenicol (inhibits 50S peptidyl transferase), macrolides/erythromycin (block 50S translocation). This selectivity is the basis of antibiotic therapy. Ricin toxin: depurinates 28S rRNA → inactivates eukaryotic ribosomes → extremely lethal.

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes the cell membrane (plasma membrane)?
    [ ] A rigid outer layer composed of cellulose that provides structural support and protection in all cell types
    [*] A selectively permeable phospholipid bilayer (described by the fluid mosaic model — Singer & Nicolson, 1972) with embedded integral proteins (channels, carriers, receptors), peripheral proteins, cholesterol (in animal cells), and glycoproteins/glycolipids — functioning as the cell's boundary that controls what enters and exits, maintains homeostasis, and facilitates cell signaling and recognition
    [ ] A dense layer of peptidoglycan found exclusively around bacterial cells that protects them from osmotic lysis
    [ ] A nuclear structure composed of lamin proteins that provides an anchor for chromatin during interphase
    Explanation: Cell membrane (plasma membrane): (1) Structure: phospholipid bilayer ~7.5-10 nm thick. Phospholipids: hydrophilic head (phosphate + glycerol) facing aqueous environment; hydrophobic tails (fatty acid chains) facing inward. (2) Fluid mosaic model (Singer & Nicolson, 1972): 'fluid' = lipids and proteins move laterally; 'mosaic' = diverse proteins in the membrane. (3) Components: integral/transmembrane proteins (span bilayer — channels, carriers, receptors, enzymes); peripheral proteins (loosely attached to surface — enzymes, cytoskeletal links); cholesterol (~20% of animal membrane lipids — modulates fluidity); glycocalyx (carbohydrate chains on glycoproteins/glycolipids — cell recognition, protection). (4) Functions: selective permeability (what enters/exits cell), cell signaling (receptors bind ligands → intracellular cascades), cell adhesion (cadherins, integrins), cell recognition (MHC, blood type antigens), endocytosis/exocytosis. (5) Transport: passive (diffusion, osmosis, facilitated diffusion — no ATP); active (primary: Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase — 3 Na⁺ out, 2 K⁺ in per ATP; secondary: cotransporters using ion gradients); bulk transport (endocytosis: phagocytosis/pinocytosis/receptor-mediated; exocytosis).

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes the endoplasmic reticulum (ER)?
    [ ] A stack of flattened cisternae that modifies, sorts, and packages proteins into transport vesicles for delivery to their final cellular destinations
    [*] An extensive network of interconnected membrane-bound tubules and flattened sacs continuous with the nuclear envelope — existing in two forms: rough ER (studded with ribosomes, synthesizing and processing secretory and membrane proteins, performing N-linked glycosylation and quality control) and smooth ER (lacking ribosomes, synthesizing lipids and steroid hormones, detoxifying drugs via cytochrome P450, and storing/releasing calcium ions)
    [ ] A small, dense body within the nucleus that synthesizes ribosomal RNA and assembles ribosome subunits before they are exported to the cytoplasm
    [ ] A set of hollow protein tubes composed of tubulin dimers that form the mitotic spindle and serve as tracks for motor protein transport
    Explanation: Endoplasmic reticulum: (1) Largest membrane-bound organelle (>50% of total cell membrane in some cells). Continuous with outer nuclear membrane. (2) Rough ER (RER): ribosomes on cytoplasmic surface. Functions: co-translational translocation (signal peptide → SRP → ER translocon → polypeptide enters ER lumen), protein folding (chaperones: BiP/GRP78, calnexin/calreticulin), N-linked glycosylation (core oligosaccharide Glc₃Man₉GlcNAc₂ transferred to Asn-X-Ser/Thr), disulfide bond formation (PDI — protein disulfide isomerase), quality control (ERAD: ER-associated degradation of misfolded proteins → retrotranslocated to cytoplasm → ubiquitinated → proteasome). (3) Smooth ER (SER): no ribosomes. Functions: phospholipid synthesis (phosphatidylcholine, phosphatidylethanolamine, phosphatidylserine — flippases transfer to inner leaflet), steroid hormone synthesis (abundant SER in adrenal cortex and gonads), detoxification (cytochrome P450 monooxygenases in liver — oxidize drugs, toxins, ethanol; chronic alcohol → SER proliferation → increased drug tolerance), calcium storage/release (sarcoplasmic reticulum = specialized SER in muscle: Ca²⁺ released via ryanodine receptors → muscle contraction; SERCA pump returns Ca²⁺). (4) ER stress and unfolded protein response (UPR): accumulation of unfolded proteins activates three pathways (IRE1, PERK, ATF6) → increase chaperone production, reduce protein translation, activate ERAD. If overwhelmed → apoptosis.

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes the Golgi apparatus?
    [ ] A double-membrane organelle that produces ATP by oxidizing pyruvate through the citric acid cycle and electron transport chain
    [*] A membrane-bound organelle consisting of stacked, flattened cisternae (discovered by Camillo Golgi, 1898) that receives proteins from the ER, further modifies them (glycosylation, phosphorylation, sulfation, proteolytic cleavage), sorts them using molecular address tags (e.g., mannose-6-phosphate for lysosomal targeting), and packages them into vesicles for delivery to the plasma membrane, lysosomes, or secretion — functioning as the cell's processing, sorting, and shipping center
    [ ] A non-membrane-bound structure composed of rRNA and protein that translates mRNA into polypeptide chains during protein synthesis
    [ ] A network of actin filaments beneath the cell membrane that provides structural support and enables cell crawling movement
    Explanation: Golgi apparatus — processing, sorting, shipping: (1) Structure: stack of 4-8 flattened membrane cisternae. Polarized: cis face (receiving, near ER) → medial → trans face (shipping, near plasma membrane) → trans-Golgi network (TGN — sorting hub). (2) Traffic: COPII vesicles from ER → cis-Golgi. Proteins move through cisternae (cisternal maturation model or vesicular transport model — likely both occur). COPI vesicles: retrograde transport Golgi → ER (retrieves escaped ER proteins with KDEL signal). (3) Modifications: glycosylation (N-linked: trimmed and rebuilt; O-linked: added de novo to Ser/Thr), phosphorylation (mannose-6-phosphate tagging by GlcNAc phosphotransferase → M6P receptor → lysosomal delivery), sulfation (tyrosine residues, GAGs), proteolytic processing (proinsulin → insulin + C-peptide; pro-opiomelanocortin → ACTH + β-endorphin). (4) Sorting and export: constitutive secretion (default — continuous); regulated secretion (stored in secretory granules, released on signal: insulin from β-cells, neurotransmitters from neurons). (5) Clinical: I-cell disease/mucolipidosis II (GlcNAc phosphotransferase deficiency → no M6P tag → lysosomal enzymes secreted → cellular waste accumulates → severe intellectual disability, skeletal abnormalities, death in childhood).

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes a lysosome?
    [ ] An energy-producing organelle with cristae-folded inner membranes that contains its own DNA and ribosomes
    [*] A single-membrane-bound organelle (discovered by Christian de Duve, Nobel 1974) containing approximately 50 types of acid hydrolases (enzymes active at pH ~4.5-5.0, maintained by H⁺-ATPase proton pumps) that digest worn-out organelles (autophagy), engulfed pathogens and particles (phagocytosis/endocytosis), and macromolecules — with genetic deficiencies in specific lysosomal enzymes causing lysosomal storage diseases (e.g., Tay-Sachs, Gaucher disease)
    [ ] A smooth, ribosome-free membrane network that synthesizes phospholipids, detoxifies drugs, and stores calcium ions for muscle contraction
    [ ] A protein complex embedded in the inner mitochondrial membrane that uses a proton gradient to synthesize ATP from ADP and inorganic phosphate
    Explanation: Lysosome — cellular digestive system: (1) Discovery: Christian de Duve (Nobel Prize 1974) — discovered through cell fractionation (differential centrifugation) studying acid phosphatase. Coined 'lysosome' from Greek: lysis (loosening) + soma (body). (2) Structure: single membrane. Interior pH 4.5-5.0 (vs. cytoplasmic pH 7.2) maintained by vacuolar H⁺-ATPase proton pumps (V-ATPase). Safety mechanism: if lysosome ruptures, acid hydrolases are inactive at cytoplasmic pH. (3) ~50+ hydrolytic enzymes: proteases (cathepsins), lipases, nucleases, glycosidases, phosphatases, sulfatases. All are acid hydrolases with optimal activity at pH ~5. (4) Pathways: autophagy (damaged organelles/proteins enclosed by autophagosome → fuses with lysosome → autolysosome → digestion; Ohsumi, Nobel 2016), phagocytosis (immune cells: phagosome + lysosome → phagolysosome), endocytosis (receptor-mediated — e.g., LDL cholesterol uptake: LDL receptor → clathrin-coated pit → endosome → lysosome → cholesterol released). (5) Lysosomal storage diseases (~50 types, ~1/7,000 births collectively): Tay-Sachs (hexosaminidase A deficiency), Gaucher (glucocerebrosidase — most common, treatable with ERT), Fabry (α-galactosidase A), Pompe (acid maltase/GAA), Niemann-Pick (sphingomyelinase types A/B; NPC1/NPC2 type C), Hurler syndrome (α-L-iduronidase — mucopolysaccharidosis I).

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes the cytoskeleton?
    [ ] The extracellular matrix composed of collagen fibers and proteoglycans that surrounds animal cells and provides tissue-level structural support
    [*] A dynamic network of protein filaments within the cytoplasm that provides structural support, maintains cell shape, enables cell movement, and facilitates intracellular transport — consisting of three main components: microfilaments (actin, ~7 nm — cell shape, muscle contraction, cytokinesis), microtubules (tubulin, ~25 nm — mitotic spindle, cilia/flagella, intracellular transport via kinesin/dynein), and intermediate filaments (~10 nm — e.g., keratin, vimentin — mechanical strength)
    [ ] A set of enzymes within the mitochondrial matrix that oxidize acetyl-CoA through the citric acid cycle, producing NADH and FADH₂
    [ ] The phospholipid bilayer that encloses the entire cell and separates the internal contents from the external environment
    Explanation: Cytoskeleton — dynamic cell framework: (1) Three components: microfilaments, microtubules, intermediate filaments — each with distinct structure, dynamics, and function. (2) Microfilaments (actin): ~7 nm, thinnest. G-actin monomers → F-actin polymer (double helix). Dynamic: polymerization/depolymerization (treadmilling). Functions: cortical actin (cell shape), stress fibers (cell adhesion/contractility), contractile ring (cytokinesis), lamellipodia/filopodia (cell migration), microvilli, muscle contraction (actin + myosin). Regulated by: Rho GTPases (Rho → stress fibers, Rac → lamellipodia, Cdc42 → filopodia). Drugs: cytochalasin (blocks polymerization), phalloidin (stabilizes). (3) Microtubules: ~25 nm, largest. α/β-tubulin heterodimers → hollow tubes. Emanate from MTOC/centrosome (γ-tubulin ring complex nucleates assembly). Dynamic instability: rapid switching between growth and shrinkage. Functions: mitotic spindle (chromosome segregation), intracellular transport (kinesin: + end, anterograde; dynein: − end, retrograde), cilia (9+2 axoneme — motile cilia in airways, fallopian tubes; primary/sensory cilia — signaling), flagella (sperm), cell shape, centrioles (9×3). Drugs: colchicine (blocks polymerization — gout), taxol (stabilizes — breast/ovarian cancer), vinca alkaloids (block polymerization — leukemia). (4) Intermediate filaments: ~10 nm. Most stable, least dynamic. Types: keratins (epithelial — 54 genes!), vimentin (mesenchymal), desmin (muscle), neurofilaments (neurons), lamins (nuclear). Functions: mechanical strength, tissue integrity. Mutations: epidermolysis bullosa simplex (keratin 5/14 → skin blistering).

  Q21. A pathologist examining a liver biopsy from a patient with chronic alcohol abuse notices that the smooth endoplasmic reticulum (SER) in hepatocytes is dramatically enlarged — nearly three times the normal amount. The patient also reports needing higher doses of prescription medications to achieve the same effect. Using your knowledge of cell organelles, explain the connection between alcohol consumption, SER proliferation, and drug tolerance.
    [ ] The SER has enlarged because it is storing excess alcohol as fat droplets within its membranes — the drug tolerance is unrelated and is caused by decreased stomach absorption of medications
    [*] The SER in liver cells contains cytochrome P450 detoxification enzymes that metabolize alcohol and drugs — chronic alcohol exposure stimulates SER proliferation (the cell makes more SER to increase detoxification capacity), which increases the total amount of P450 enzymes — these same enzymes also metabolize prescription drugs, so more SER means faster drug breakdown, requiring higher doses to maintain therapeutic blood levels — this is cross-tolerance through organelle adaptation
    [ ] The enlarged SER is producing excess ribosomes to replace those damaged by alcohol — the drug tolerance occurs because these extra ribosomes produce antibodies that neutralize medications
    [ ] Alcohol directly destroys the rough ER, and the smooth ER enlarges to compensate by taking over protein synthesis — drug tolerance is caused by the liver running out of ATP to metabolize medications
    Explanation: SER proliferation and drug tolerance: (1) SER detoxification function: liver hepatocytes contain abundant SER with cytochrome P450 (CYP) enzymes — a superfamily of monooxygenases. CYP2E1 primarily metabolizes ethanol (also acetaminophen, anesthetics). CYP3A4 metabolizes ~50% of all prescription drugs. CYP2D6 metabolizes ~25% of drugs. (2) Enzyme induction: chronic alcohol exposure → gene upregulation → increased CYP enzyme synthesis → SER membrane proliferation to accommodate more enzymes. SER surface area can increase 2-3× in chronic alcoholics. (3) Cross-tolerance mechanism: more CYP enzymes → faster metabolism of ALL CYP substrates (not just alcohol) → drugs cleared faster → lower blood levels → reduced therapeutic effect → need higher doses. Clinically significant: alcoholics may need higher doses of anesthetics, sedatives, antidepressants, anticoagulants. (4) Dangerous interaction: acetaminophen (Tylenol) is metabolized by CYP2E1 to toxic NAPQI (normally detoxified by glutathione). Induced CYP2E1 in alcoholics → more NAPQI production + alcohol depletes glutathione → NAPQI accumulates → severe hepatotoxicity. Acetaminophen overdose + alcohol = leading cause of acute liver failure. (5) This demonstrates a fundamental cell biology principle: organelle quantity adapts to functional demand (organelle biogenesis is regulated).

  Q22. A child is diagnosed with Tay-Sachs disease after developing progressive neurological deterioration at 6 months of age. Genetic testing confirms a mutation in the HEXA gene encoding the lysosomal enzyme hexosaminidase A. Explain how this single enzyme deficiency leads to the devastating symptoms, and what this tells us about lysosomal function.
    [ ] The missing enzyme normally produces myelin sheaths around neurons — without it, neurons cannot conduct electrical signals, causing immediate paralysis from birth
    [*] Without functional hexosaminidase A, the lipid GM2 ganglioside cannot be broken down in lysosomes — it accumulates progressively in neurons (which have high ganglioside turnover), swelling lysosomes to the point where they physically distort and destroy the neurons — this demonstrates that lysosomes must continuously digest cellular waste, and even one missing enzyme among the ~50 hydrolases can cause catastrophic accumulation because no other enzyme can compensate for the specific substrate
    [ ] Hexosaminidase A is a mitochondrial enzyme needed for ATP production — without it, neurons lack energy and gradually die from starvation, which has nothing to do with lysosomal function
    [ ] The HEXA mutation causes the ribosomes in neurons to malfunction, producing toxic proteins that damage the cell membrane — lysosomes are not involved in the disease mechanism
    Explanation: Tay-Sachs disease — lysosomal storage disease paradigm: (1) Genetics: autosomal recessive. HEXA gene (chromosome 15) encodes α-subunit of hexosaminidase A (a heterodimer: α + β subunits). Carrier frequency: ~1/30 in Ashkenazi Jewish, French-Canadian, and Cajun populations (historical founder effects + possible heterozygote advantage against tuberculosis). (2) Biochemistry: hexosaminidase A normally cleaves terminal N-acetylgalactosamine from GM2 ganglioside in lysosomes → GM2 → GM3 → further degradation. Without functional enzyme → GM2 ganglioside accumulates. (3) Why neurons? Gangliosides are especially abundant in neuronal membranes (constitute ~6% of brain lipids). High ganglioside turnover → rapid accumulation of undegraded GM2. Lysosomes swell with membranous cytoplasmic bodies (MCBs) → physical distortion of neuronal architecture → axonal dysfunction → neuronal death. (4) Clinical: infantile form (most severe, >90% of cases): normal at birth; developmental regression at ~6 months; cherry-red spot on retinal exam (fovea appears red because surrounding ganglion cells are swollen/pale with lipid); progressive blindness, deafness, seizures; death by age 4-5. No cure; enzyme replacement therapy doesn't cross blood-brain barrier effectively. (5) Lesson: demonstrates lysosomal enzyme specificity — each of ~50 hydrolases has unique substrate(s), and deficiency of any one enzyme causes specific substrate accumulation → specific disease. Also illustrates: organelle dysfunction → cell dysfunction → tissue dysfunction → organismal disease.

  Q23. A cancer researcher discovers that a new chemotherapy drug works by stabilizing microtubules, preventing them from depolymerizing. She predicts this will stop cancer cells from dividing. Her colleague argues that the drug should destabilize microtubules instead. Explain why BOTH approaches (stabilizing and destabilizing microtubules) would effectively block cell division.
    [ ] Only destabilizing microtubules would work — stable microtubules would help the cell divide faster because the mitotic spindle would be stronger
    [*] Cell division requires dynamic instability of microtubules — the mitotic spindle must polymerize to reach and attach to kinetochores, then depolymerize to pull chromosomes apart — stabilizing drugs (like taxol/paclitaxel) prevent depolymerization so chromosomes cannot be separated, while destabilizing drugs (like vincristine/colchicine) prevent polymerization so the spindle cannot form at all — both approaches arrest cells at metaphase, activating the spindle assembly checkpoint and triggering apoptosis
    [ ] Neither approach would work because cell division depends entirely on actin microfilaments, not microtubules — the mitotic spindle is made of actin
    [ ] Both approaches would work, but only because they both damage the cell membrane, causing the cell to lyse before it can complete division
    Explanation: Microtubule-targeting anticancer drugs: (1) Dynamic instability is essential: microtubules in the mitotic spindle switch rapidly between growth (polymerization) and shrinkage (depolymerization) — this 'search and capture' mechanism allows spindle fibers to find and attach to kinetochores on chromosomes. After attachment, depolymerization generates the pulling force to separate sister chromatids. (2) Stabilizing drugs (prevent depolymerization): Taxol/paclitaxel (from Pacific yew tree bark, Taxus brevifolia; Bristol-Myers Squibb) — binds β-tubulin inside microtubule → prevents disassembly. Epothilones (epothilone B/ixabepilone). Result: spindle forms but cannot pull chromosomes → metaphase arrest → spindle assembly checkpoint (SAC) activated → prolonged arrest → apoptosis. Used for: breast, ovarian, lung, pancreatic cancers. (3) Destabilizing drugs (prevent polymerization): Vinca alkaloids — vincristine, vinblastine (from Catharanthus roseus, Madagascar periwinkle) — bind tubulin dimers → prevent polymerization; colchicine (from Colchicum autumnale, autumn crocus) — blocks polymerization. Result: spindle cannot form → chromosomes cannot align → metaphase arrest → SAC → apoptosis. Vincristine: childhood leukemia (ALL). Vinblastine: Hodgkin lymphoma, testicular cancer. (4) Key insight: it's not the presence or absence of microtubules that matters — it's the dynamic cycling between assembly and disassembly. Freezing microtubules in either state (too stable or too unstable) is equally lethal to dividing cells. (5) Selectivity: cancer cells divide more frequently than most normal cells → more susceptible to mitotic poisons. Side effects affect other rapidly dividing cells (hair follicles → hair loss, gut epithelium → nausea, bone marrow → immunosuppression) and neurons (peripheral neuropathy).

  Q24. A genetic counselor explains to a couple that their unborn child has been diagnosed with I-cell disease (mucolipidosis II). She describes it as a defect in the Golgi apparatus's ability to properly tag proteins with mannose-6-phosphate. The couple asks why a 'tagging' problem could be so devastating. Explain the molecular mechanism and its consequences.
    [ ] Mannose-6-phosphate is a sugar added to proteins to make them taste sweet to the cell — without this tag, the cell rejects the proteins and they are destroyed harmlessly
    [*] In I-cell disease, the enzyme GlcNAc phosphotransferase (which adds mannose-6-phosphate tags in the Golgi) is deficient — without M6P tags, lysosomal enzymes are not recognized by M6P receptors in the trans-Golgi network and instead follow the default secretory pathway out of the cell — lysosomes receive no digestive enzymes and cannot break down cellular waste — undigested macromolecules accumulate as dense inclusion bodies (hence 'I-cell'), causing severe skeletal abnormalities, intellectual disability, and death in early childhood
    [ ] Mannose-6-phosphate is needed for DNA replication in the nucleus — without it, cells cannot divide and the child will simply grow more slowly but otherwise be healthy
    [ ] The tagging problem only affects bacteria inside the child's gut — I-cell disease is actually caused by antibiotic-resistant bacteria that cannot be tagged for immune destruction
    Explanation: I-cell disease — Golgi sorting failure: (1) Normal pathway: lysosomal enzymes are synthesized in RER → transported to Golgi → in cis-Golgi, GlcNAc phosphotransferase recognizes a signal patch on lysosomal enzyme → adds GlcNAc-phosphate to mannose residues → phosphodiesterase removes GlcNAc → exposed mannose-6-phosphate (M6P). In trans-Golgi network: M6P receptors bind M6P-tagged enzymes → clathrin-coated vesicles → late endosomes → lysosomes. Acidic pH causes enzyme release from M6P receptor → receptor recycled. (2) I-cell disease: GlcNAc phosphotransferase deficiency (GNPTAB gene mutations) → lysosomal enzymes lack M6P tag → not recognized by M6P receptors → follow default constitutive secretory pathway → secreted outside cell. Diagnostic: elevated lysosomal enzyme levels in blood/serum (because they're being secreted); deficient enzyme activity inside cells (because lysosomes are empty). (3) Consequences: lysosomes cannot degrade macromolecules → accumulation of undigested substrates → dense inclusion bodies in cells (visible under phase-contrast microscopy → 'inclusion cell' disease). Affects virtually all cell types. (4) Clinical: coarse facial features, skeletal dysplasia (dysostosis multiplex), severe psychomotor retardation, corneal clouding, gingival hyperplasia, hepatosplenomegaly. Usually fatal by age 5-8. (5) Demonstrates: the critical importance of protein sorting in the Golgi — even though cells produce the needed enzymes, they're useless if delivered to the wrong destination.

  Q25. A microbiologist isolates a mysterious single-celled organism from a deep-sea hydrothermal vent. Initial examination shows it is 0.8 μm in diameter, has no visible nucleus, and can survive at 110°C. However, further analysis reveals that its membrane contains ether-linked lipids and it has histone-like proteins bound to its circular DNA. How would you classify this organism, and why doesn't it fit neatly into the 'prokaryote' category as traditionally understood?
    [ ] It must be a eukaryote because it has histone-like proteins — all organisms with any histone proteins have a nucleus, and the microscopist must have missed it
    [*] This organism is likely an archaeon — archaea are prokaryotic in structure (no nucleus, small size, circular DNA) but share several molecular features with eukaryotes, including histone-like proteins, some eukaryotic-type transcription/translation machinery, and ether-linked lipids (unique to archaea, vs. ester-linked in bacteria) — this is why the three-domain system (Woese, 1990) separates Bacteria and Archaea, recognizing that 'prokaryote' lumps together two fundamentally different groups, and archaea are actually more closely related to eukaryotes than to bacteria
    [ ] It is a very small plant cell that has lost its chloroplasts due to the absence of light at hydrothermal vents — the histone proteins confirm it is a plant
    [ ] It must be a virus since it is smaller than 1 μm and lacks a nucleus — viruses commonly have histone-like proteins and ether-linked lipids
    Explanation: Archaea and the three domains: (1) Carl Woese (1977) used 16S rRNA sequencing to discover that 'prokaryotes' are actually two fundamentally different groups: Bacteria and Archaea. Led to the three-domain system (1990): Bacteria, Archaea, Eukarya. (2) Archaea — prokaryotic structure: no nuclear membrane, no membrane-bound organelles, small size (0.1-15 μm), circular chromosome(s), binary fission. BUT molecular features are a mosaic of bacterial AND eukaryotic traits. (3) Archaeal features shared with eukaryotes (not bacteria): histone-like proteins (archaeal histones wrap DNA similarly to eukaryotic nucleosomes), RNA polymerase similar to eukaryotic RNA Pol II (multi-subunit), TATA-binding protein (TBP) in transcription initiation, translation initiation with methionine (not formyl-methionine as in bacteria), some introns in tRNA genes. (4) Unique archaeal features: ether-linked lipids (isoprenoid chains linked to glycerol via ether bonds, vs. ester bonds in bacteria/eukaryotes → more heat-stable), some form lipid monolayers (tetraether lipids spanning entire membrane — extremely stable at high temperatures), unique cell wall composition (no peptidoglycan — pseudopeptidoglycan, S-layer, or none). (5) Extremophile archaea: thermophiles (Pyrolobus fumarii — up to 113°C), halophiles (Halobacterium — 5M NaCl), acidophiles (Sulfolobus — pH 1-2), methanogens (Methanobacterium — strict anaerobes, produce CH₄). But archaea also live in 'normal' environments — abundant in ocean water, soil, human gut. (6) Phylogenetic implication: Archaea + Eukaryota form a clade to the exclusion of Bacteria. Eukaryotes likely evolved from an archaeal ancestor (Asgard archaea — Lokiarchaeota discovered 2015 — closest known archaeal relatives of eukaryotes).

  Q26. A patient with primary ciliary dyskinesia (PCD, also called Kartagener syndrome when accompanied by situs inversus) suffers from chronic respiratory infections, infertility, and has a 50% chance of having mirror-reversed organ placement. His physician explains that all these symptoms result from defective cilia. How can one structural defect — in a cytoskeletal component — cause such diverse symptoms across different organ systems?
    [ ] PCD only affects the lungs — the infertility and organ reversal are caused by separate, unrelated genetic mutations that happen to occur together frequently
    [*] PCD is caused by mutations in dynein motor proteins (usually outer dynein arms) of the 9+2 microtubule axoneme in motile cilia — defective respiratory cilia cannot clear mucus from airways (→ chronic infections/bronchiectasis), defective flagella on sperm cannot propel them (→ male infertility), defective cilia in fallopian tubes cannot move the egg (→ female subfertility), and defective nodal cilia in the embryo cannot generate the left-right fluid flow that determines organ asymmetry (→ 50% random chance of situs inversus — mirror-image organs)
    [ ] PCD is caused by excess actin microfilaments clogging the airways — the organ reversal happens because microfilaments push organs to the wrong side during development
    [ ] PCD is caused by overactive lysosomes digesting the respiratory epithelium — the cilia are normal but the cells beneath them are destroyed
    Explanation: Primary ciliary dyskinesia — one cytoskeletal defect, many organ systems: (1) Motile cilia structure: 9+2 axoneme (9 outer doublet microtubules + 2 central singlets). Outer and inner dynein arms (molecular motors) on outer doublets generate sliding force → bending. Nexin links and radial spokes coordinate bending → rhythmic beating pattern (~10-20 Hz). (2) PCD genetics: autosomal recessive. ~50 genes known. Most common: DNAI1, DNAH5 (outer dynein arm components). ~1/15,000-20,000 births. (3) Respiratory consequences: airway cilia (in trachea, bronchi — mucociliary escalator) beat in coordinated waves to move mucus + trapped particles upward toward pharynx → clearance. Defective cilia → mucus stagnation → bacterial colonization → chronic sinusitis, bronchitis, bronchiectasis (permanent airway dilation), otitis media. (4) Fertility: sperm flagellum has 9+2 axoneme (same dynein motors) → immotile sperm → male infertility. Fallopian tube cilia move ovum → female subfertility. (5) Situs inversus (Kartagener syndrome, ~50% of PCD): during embryonic development (day 7-8), nodal cilia at the embryonic node rotate clockwise → create leftward fluid flow → asymmetric gene expression (Nodal, Lefty, Pitx2) → normal left-right organ placement (situs solitus). Immotile nodal cilia → no directional flow → organ placement is random (50:50 normal or mirror-image). Most situs inversus patients are clinically normal (mirror-image organs work fine) unless accompanied by cardiac defects (heterotaxy). (6) Demonstrates: the cytoskeleton isn't just structural — microtubule-based motility is essential for mucus clearance, reproduction, and embryonic development.

  Q27. An evolutionary biologist compares the genomes of mitochondria and the free-living bacterium Rickettsia prowazekii (which causes epidemic typhus). She finds remarkable similarities: both have circular DNA, similar gene sequences, and both encode components of the electron transport chain. However, the mitochondrial genome is much smaller (~16,500 bp vs. ~1.1 million bp). What does this evidence tell us about the origin of mitochondria, and why has the mitochondrial genome shrunk?
    [ ] The similarities are purely coincidental — mitochondria and Rickettsia evolved independently, and circular DNA is found in all organisms, making it a meaningless comparison
    [*] This evidence strongly supports the endosymbiotic theory (Lynn Margulis, 1967): mitochondria descended from a free-living α-proteobacterium (related to modern Rickettsia) that was engulfed by an ancestral eukaryotic cell ~1.5-2 billion years ago — the mitochondrial genome shrank because most genes were transferred to the host cell's nuclear DNA over evolutionary time (endosymbiotic gene transfer), leaving only 37 genes in human mtDNA — the mitochondrion became dependent on the host for most proteins, which are now imported via the TOM/TIM translocation complexes
    [ ] Mitochondria are actually modified peroxisomes that acquired DNA from Rickettsia through horizontal gene transfer in the modern era — they have no ancient evolutionary origin
    [ ] The smaller mitochondrial genome proves that mitochondria are degenerate viruses that lost most of their genes after infecting eukaryotic cells — they are parasites, not symbionts
    Explanation: Endosymbiotic theory and mitochondrial genome evolution: (1) Endosymbiotic theory (Lynn Margulis, 1967; originally proposed by Konstantin Mereschkowski, 1905, and Ivan Wallin, 1927): an ancestral anaerobic eukaryote engulfed an aerobic α-proteobacterium → mutualistic endosymbiosis (host provided nutrients, endosymbiont provided efficient aerobic ATP production). Over ~1.5-2 billion years → obligate symbiosis → mitochondrion. (2) Evidence: double membrane (inner = original bacterial membrane, outer = host's phagosomal membrane); circular DNA (like bacteria); 70S ribosomes (like bacteria, not eukaryotic 80S); binary fission-like division (independent of cell cycle); cardiolipin in inner membrane (bacterial membrane lipid); phylogenetics: mitochondrial rRNA sequences cluster within α-proteobacteria, closest to Rickettsiales. (3) Genome reduction: human mtDNA = 16,569 bp (37 genes: 13 proteins for ETC/ATP synthase + 22 tRNAs + 2 rRNAs). Rickettsia prowazekii = ~1.1 Mb. α-proteobacterial ancestor likely had ~3,000-4,000 genes. (4) Endosymbiotic gene transfer (EGT): over evolutionary time, most mitochondrial genes were transferred to the nuclear genome. ~1,500 nuclear-encoded proteins are now imported into mitochondria via TOM (translocase of outer membrane) and TIM (translocase of inner membrane) complexes, using N-terminal targeting sequences (mitochondrial targeting peptides). (5) Why retain any genes? Some highly hydrophobic inner membrane proteins may be difficult to import from cytoplasm. Co-location for redox regulation (CoRR) hypothesis: keeping genes in mitochondria allows local regulation of gene expression in response to respiratory chain status.

  Q28. A research team generates the first complete synthetic bacterial genome (Craig Venter's team, 2010 — Mycoplasma mycoides JCVI-syn1.0) and transplants it into a different species of Mycoplasma whose own genome has been removed. The cell with the synthetic genome boots up, begins transcribing genes, and divides. Some commentators call this 'creating life.' Using your knowledge of cell theory and cell structure, evaluate this claim.
    [ ] This clearly created life from scratch — since DNA is the only component needed for a cell, inserting synthetic DNA into an empty lipid vesicle would have worked equally well
    [*] This did NOT create life from non-living matter — the synthetic genome was inserted into an existing cell that already had all the necessary cellular machinery (ribosomes, enzymes, membranes, metabolites, ATP, tRNAs) — cell theory states all cells come from pre-existing cells, and this experiment still required a pre-existing cell as the 'operating system' to read and execute the new genomic 'software' — it demonstrated that DNA is the informational blueprint, but a cell is far more than its genome
    [ ] This experiment disproved cell theory by showing that cells can arise from non-living synthetic chemicals — it proved that spontaneous generation is possible under laboratory conditions
    [ ] The experiment failed because the transplanted genome was immediately destroyed by the host cell's immune system — Mycoplasma cells have restriction enzymes that digest any foreign DNA
    Explanation: Synthetic biology and cell theory: (1) Craig Venter's experiment (2010, Science): synthesized the complete 1.08 Mb genome of Mycoplasma mycoides from chemically synthesized oligonucleotides → assembled in yeast → transplanted into Mycoplasma capricolum recipient cell whose own genome had been removed. The 'rebooted' cell expressed the synthetic M. mycoides genome, produced M. mycoides proteins, and divided → established a self-replicating strain (JCVI-syn1.0). (2) What it demonstrated: DNA is the software that directs cellular behavior. Even a completely synthetic genome can direct a living cell. Genome transplantation effectively converts one species into another. (3) What it did NOT demonstrate: creation of life from scratch. The experiment absolutely required a pre-existing cell with intact: ribosomes (to translate the new genome), RNA polymerase (to transcribe it), ATP and metabolites (to power initial gene expression), the entire membrane system (to contain everything), tRNAs, amino acids, nucleotides, etc. (4) Cell theory analysis: tenet 3 ('all cells from pre-existing cells') was upheld — the synthetic cell arose from a pre-existing cell. The genome was new, but the cell was not. A thought experiment: if you put the synthetic genome into a test tube with only water and salts — nothing would happen. The cell is the minimal unit of life, not the genome alone. (5) Later work: JCVI-syn3.0 (2016) — minimal genome of 473 genes (149 of unknown function!) — the smallest genome that can sustain independent life. Still required a recipient cell. (6) Philosophical: the experiment blurs the line between natural and artificial but reinforces, rather than refutes, cell theory.

  Q29. An immunologist explains to medical students how macrophages destroy bacteria using an elegant coordination of multiple organelles. A macrophage engulfs a Staphylococcus aureus bacterium. Trace the subsequent events, identifying each organelle involved and its role in the process.
    [ ] The bacterium enters the nucleus where DNA enzymes destroy it — then the mitochondria digest the remains and excrete them through the cell membrane
    [*] The macrophage extends pseudopodia (actin cytoskeleton) to engulf the bacterium into a phagosome (membrane-bound vesicle) → the phagosome fuses with a lysosome to form a phagolysosome → lysosomal acid hydrolases (pH ~4.5) digest the bacterium's proteins, lipids, carbohydrates, and nucleic acids → bacterial antigens are loaded onto MHC II molecules (processed in the ER and Golgi) and presented on the cell surface → meanwhile, the Golgi packages and delivers new lysosomal enzymes and membrane components, mitochondria provide ATP for the energy-demanding process, and the ER synthesizes new membrane and proteins
    [ ] The bacterium is immediately killed by the acidity of the cytoplasm (pH 2.0) — no specific organelles are involved in the process, and the dead bacterium simply dissolves in the cytosol
    [ ] Smooth ER wraps around the bacterium and uses cytochrome P450 enzymes to oxidize it — the Golgi then packages the oxidized remains into secretory vesicles that are expelled from the cell
    Explanation: Phagocytosis — organelle coordination in immune defense: (1) Recognition: macrophage receptors (Toll-like receptors/TLRs, Fc receptors, complement receptors, mannose receptors) bind PAMPs on bacterial surface or opsonins (antibodies, complement C3b). (2) Engulfment: actin polymerization (cytoskeleton) drives pseudopod extension → bacterium enclosed in phagosome (membrane-bound vacuole derived from plasma membrane). Energy: mitochondrial ATP powers actin dynamics and membrane remodeling. (3) Phagosome maturation: early phagosome (Rab5 GTPase, slight acidification) → late phagosome (Rab7, more acidification by V-ATPase) → phagolysosomе (fusion with lysosome). Fusion mediated by SNARE proteins, Rab GTPases. (4) Killing and digestion in phagolysosome: pH drops to ~4.5 (V-ATPase proton pumps). Acid hydrolases: cathepsins (proteases), lysozyme (cleaves peptidoglycan), DNase/RNase, lipases, glycosidases. Oxidative burst: NADPH oxidase → superoxide (O₂⁻•) → H₂O₂ → HOCl (myeloperoxidase) — extremely bactericidal. iNOS → nitric oxide (NO•) → peroxynitrite (ONOO⁻). (5) Antigen presentation: bacterial protein fragments (peptides, 13-25 amino acids) loaded onto MHC class II molecules (assembled in ER, processed in Golgi) → transported to cell surface → presented to CD4⁺ helper T cells → adaptive immune response initiation. ER and Golgi are essential for MHC synthesis and processing. (6) Waste disposal: indigested remnants expelled by exocytosis. (7) Summary of organelles: cell membrane (recognition/engulfment), cytoskeleton-actin (pseudopodia), lysosomes (digestion), mitochondria (ATP), ER (MHC/protein synthesis), Golgi (MHC processing/lysosomal enzyme delivery), endosomes (maturation pathway).

  Q30. A cell biologist discovers that a rare genetic mutation causes a patient's cells to completely lack peroxisomes (Zellweger spectrum disorder). The patient, an infant, has severe neurological dysfunction, liver disease, and abnormal facial features. Why would the absence of this single, seemingly minor organelle cause such devastating multi-organ failure?
    [ ] Peroxisomes are the primary site of protein synthesis — without them, cells cannot make any proteins and all organ systems fail simultaneously
    [*] Peroxisomes perform several essential metabolic functions that no other organelle can fully compensate for: (1) very-long-chain fatty acid (VLCFA) β-oxidation (C22+ fatty acids can only be shortened in peroxisomes before mitochondria can process them), (2) synthesis of plasmalogens (ether-phospholipids that constitute ~18% of total phospholipids in the brain and are essential for myelin formation), (3) bile acid synthesis, (4) hydrogen peroxide metabolism (catalase: 2H₂O₂ → 2H₂O + O₂) — without peroxisomes, VLCFAs accumulate (damaging myelin and neurons), plasmalogens are absent (defective myelin → neurological dysfunction), bile synthesis fails (liver disease), and H₂O₂ accumulates (oxidative damage)
    [ ] Peroxisomes are needed only for detoxifying alcohol — the infant's disease is actually caused by accidental alcohol exposure, not a genetic defect in peroxisomes
    [ ] Peroxisomes are the organelles that produce all steroid hormones — without them, the infant has no cortisol, estrogen, or testosterone, causing hormonal collapse
    Explanation: Zellweger spectrum disorder — peroxisomal absence: (1) Peroxisomes: single-membrane organelles (~0.1-1 μm). Named for hydrogen peroxide (H₂O₂) — many peroxisomal oxidases produce H₂O₂ as byproduct → catalase breaks it down. ~50 enzymes. (2) Essential functions: VLCFA β-oxidation (fatty acids >C22 can ONLY be shortened in peroxisomes; after shortening to ~C8, acetyl-CoA units transferred to mitochondria for further oxidation). Plasmalogen synthesis (ether-phospholipids — ~18% of brain phospholipids, critical component of myelin — the insulating sheath around axons). α-oxidation of branched-chain fatty acids (phytanic acid from dairy/meat). Bile acid synthesis (side-chain oxidation of cholesterol). Amino acid metabolism (D-amino acid oxidase, L-pipecolic acid oxidase). H₂O₂ detoxification (catalase — one of the fastest enzymes known: ~40 million reactions/sec). (3) Zellweger syndrome (most severe of Zellweger spectrum): caused by mutations in PEX genes (peroxins — ~16 known) needed for peroxisome biogenesis/assembly. Most common: PEX1 mutations (~60% of cases). Autosomal recessive. ~1/50,000 births. (4) Pathology: VLCFA accumulation (C26:0 elevated in blood — diagnostic biomarker) → disrupts cell membranes, especially myelin → demyelination, neuronal migration defects → severe intellectual disability, seizures, hypotonia. Plasmalogen deficiency → defective myelin formation → compounds neurological damage. Bile acid synthetic intermediates accumulate → liver dysfunction (hepatomegaly, jaundice, fibrosis). Brain MRI: polymicrogyria (abnormal cortical gyral pattern), demyelination, germinolytic cysts. (5) Prognosis: most severe form (Zellweger syndrome) → death within 1st year. Milder forms (neonatal adrenoleukodystrophy, infantile Refsum disease): variable survival. No cure; treatment is supportive. (6) Demonstrates: 'minor' organelles perform unique, irreplaceable metabolic functions. The cell is an integrated system — removing any component impacts the whole.
