--- Lesson 7.5:  (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Structures and Organelles
Key Concepts: Structures and Organelles

This lesson covers the fundamental concepts of structures and organelles in biology.

  • Understanding the basic principles and terminology
  • Exploring real-world applications and examples
  • Connecting to other biological concepts

Learning Objectives

  • Define key terms related to structures and organelles
  • Explain the importance of this topic in biology
  • Apply concepts to solve problems

Why This Matters

  • Builds foundation for advanced biology topics
  • Has practical applications in medicine and research
  • Helps explain natural phenomena

--- Lesson 7.5:  (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What are the two types of endoplasmic reticulum, and how do their structures relate to their functions?
    [ ] Both rough and smooth ER are identical in structure and function — they both produce lipids and have ribosomes on their surface
    [*] Rough ER (RER) has ribosomes studding its outer surface and functions in synthesis, folding, and modification of membrane-bound and secretory proteins — Smooth ER (SER) lacks ribosomes and functions in lipid (phospholipid, steroid hormone) synthesis, calcium ion storage, and detoxification of drugs and poisons — George Palade (Nobel Prize 1974) used electron microscopy and cell fractionation to elucidate ER structure and the secretory pathway
    [ ] Rough ER produces lipids because phospholipids are rough in texture, while smooth ER produces proteins because protein surfaces are smooth
    [ ] The endoplasmic reticulum is found only in plant cells and has no equivalent in animal cells
    Explanation: Endoplasmic reticulum (ER) — two types: (1) Rough ER (RER): studded with ribosomes on cytoplasmic face. Function: synthesizes proteins destined for membranes, secretion, or lysosomes. Signal sequence on nascent polypeptide → signal recognition particle (SRP) → docks at SRP receptor on RER → protein threaded into ER lumen via translocon → folding (chaperones like BiP, calnexin) → glycosylation (N-linked sugars added) → quality control → transport vesicles bud off → Golgi. (2) Smooth ER (SER): no ribosomes. Functions: phospholipid synthesis (all membranes receive lipids from SER), steroid hormone synthesis (abundant in adrenal cortex, testes, ovaries), detoxification of drugs/alcohol/poisons (cytochrome P450 enzymes — abundant in liver hepatocytes → liver SER proliferates with chronic alcohol/drug exposure), calcium storage (sarcoplasmic reticulum in muscle = specialized SER). George Palade (Nobel 1974): pioneered electron microscopy of organelles and discovered the secretory pathway.

  Q2. What is the Golgi apparatus, how is it organized, and what are its functions in the cell?
    [ ] The Golgi apparatus is a single membrane sac that stores DNA and is responsible for cell division — it has no role in protein processing
    [*] The Golgi apparatus (discovered by Camillo Golgi, Nobel Prize 1906) is a stack of flattened membrane sacs (cisternae) organized with a cis face (receiving side, facing ER) and a trans face (shipping side, facing plasma membrane) — it modifies proteins and lipids (glycosylation, phosphorylation, proteolytic cleavage), sorts them by adding molecular 'address tags' (e.g., mannose-6-phosphate for lysosomal enzymes), and packages them into vesicles for transport to their destinations (plasma membrane, lysosomes, secretion)
    [ ] The Golgi is identical to the endoplasmic reticulum — they are the same organelle with different names used in different countries
    [ ] The Golgi apparatus is found only in prokaryotic cells and functions in peptidoglycan synthesis for the cell wall
    Explanation: Golgi apparatus (Golgi complex/body): (1) Discovered by Camillo Golgi (1898) using silver staining; Nobel Prize in Physiology/Medicine 1906 (shared with Ramón y Cajal). (2) Structure: stack of 4-8 flattened membrane cisternae. Polarized: cis face (receiving — near ER, receives transport vesicles from ER) → medial cisternae → trans face (shipping — facing plasma membrane, trans-Golgi network or TGN). (3) Functions: modification (O-linked glycosylation, modification of N-linked sugars from ER, phosphorylation — mannose-6-phosphate tag for lysosomal enzymes, sulfation, proteolytic processing — e.g., proinsulin → insulin), sorting (molecular address tags direct proteins to correct destinations), packaging (trans-Golgi network buds vesicles targeted to: plasma membrane for secretion or membrane insertion, lysosomes, or regulated secretory granules). (4) Vesicle trafficking: COPI vesicles (retrograde, Golgi → ER), COPII vesicles (anterograde, ER → Golgi), clathrin-coated vesicles (TGN → lysosomes, endosomes). James Rothman, Randy Schekman, Thomas Südhof — Nobel Prize 2013 for vesicle trafficking.

  Q3. What are lysosomes, how are they formed, and what roles do they play in the cell?
    [ ] Lysosomes are organelles that produce ATP by oxidizing glucose through aerobic respiration — they contain enzymes for the citric acid cycle
    [*] Lysosomes (discovered by Christian de Duve, Nobel Prize 1974) are membrane-bound organelles containing ~50 hydrolytic enzymes (proteases, lipases, nucleases, glycosidases) that operate at an acidic pH (~4.5-5.0, maintained by V-type H⁺ ATPase proton pumps) — they digest materials brought in by endocytosis (foreign material, pathogens) and by autophagy (damaged organelles, worn-out proteins) — lysosomal enzyme deficiencies cause ~50 lysosomal storage diseases (e.g., Tay-Sachs, Gaucher disease)
    [ ] Lysosomes are found only in plant cells where they function to store water and maintain turgor pressure — animal cells use different organelles for digestion
    [ ] Lysosomes are free-floating enzymes in the cytoplasm that have no membrane barrier — they digest cellular waste by direct contact
    Explanation: Lysosomes — the cell's digestive system: (1) Discovery: Christian de Duve (Nobel Prize 1974) discovered lysosomes using cell fractionation and centrifugation. (2) Formation: lysosomal enzymes synthesized on RER → glycosylated → Golgi adds mannose-6-phosphate (M6P) tag → M6P receptors in TGN sort enzymes into clathrin-coated vesicles → delivered to late endosomes → mature into lysosomes. (3) Internal environment: pH 4.5-5.0 maintained by V-type H⁺ ATPase (proton pump — active transport). Acidic pH optimal for hydrolase activity; if lysosome ruptures, enzymes inactive at cytoplasmic pH 7.2 (safety mechanism). (4) Functions: (a) Heterophagy: digestion of extracellular material from endocytosis/phagocytosis. (b) Autophagy (Yoshinori Ohsumi, Nobel 2016): degradation/recycling of damaged organelles and aggregated proteins. Autophagosome engulfs target → fuses with lysosome → digestion → components recycled. Essential for cellular homeostasis. (5) Lysosomal storage diseases (~50 known): enzyme deficiency → substrate accumulates → cell dysfunction. Tay-Sachs (hexosaminidase A deficiency → GM2 ganglioside accumulation in neurons), Gaucher (glucocerebrosidase deficiency), Pompe (acid maltase deficiency).

  Q4. What is the endomembrane system, and how do its components work together to produce, modify, and deliver proteins?
    [ ] The endomembrane system is limited to the plasma membrane and has no internal components — proteins are made and delivered without any organelle cooperation
    [*] The endomembrane system is a network of interconnected membrane-bound organelles — including the nuclear envelope, rough ER, smooth ER, Golgi apparatus, lysosomes, vesicles, and the plasma membrane — that work together in the secretory pathway: genes in the nucleus → mRNA → ribosomes on rough ER synthesize proteins → proteins folded and modified in ER lumen → COPII transport vesicles carry proteins to Golgi cis face → Golgi further modifies, sorts, and packages → trans-Golgi network sends vesicles to final destination (plasma membrane, lysosomes, or secretion)
    [ ] The endomembrane system consists only of mitochondria and chloroplasts — these are the only organelles with internal membranes
    [ ] The endomembrane system is a term used only in prokaryotic cells to describe their plasma membrane invaginations
    Explanation: The endomembrane system: a connected network of organelles related by direct physical continuity or vesicle transfer. Components: (1) Nuclear envelope: double membrane, continuous with rough ER, nuclear pores regulate traffic. (2) Rough ER: protein synthesis and initial modification. (3) Smooth ER: lipid synthesis, detox, Ca²⁺ storage. (4) Golgi apparatus: further modification, sorting, packaging. (5) Lysosomes: intracellular digestion. (6) Vesicles: transport containers between compartments. (7) Plasma membrane: receives vesicles via exocytosis, sends material in via endocytosis. The secretory pathway: nucleus (gene → mRNA) → RER (translation, folding, glycosylation) → COPII vesicles → Golgi cis face → medial Golgi → trans-Golgi network (sorting) → clathrin or secretory vesicles → destination. NOT part of endomembrane system: mitochondria, chloroplasts (have own DNA, double membranes from endosymbiosis — not derived from ER/Golgi), peroxisomes (may derive from ER but functionally independent). Nobel Prizes: Palade (1974 — secretory pathway), Rothman/Schekman/Südhof (2013 — vesicle trafficking).

  Q5. What are chloroplasts, and what evidence supports the endosymbiotic theory of their origin?
    [ ] Chloroplasts are found in all living cells and are responsible for cellular respiration — they evolved independently without any bacterial ancestor
    [*] Chloroplasts are double-membrane organelles in plant and algal cells where photosynthesis occurs (light reactions in thylakoid membranes, Calvin cycle in stroma) — the endosymbiotic theory (championed by Lynn Margulis, 1967) proposes that chloroplasts were once free-living photosynthetic cyanobacteria engulfed by an ancestral eukaryotic cell — evidence includes: double membranes, own circular DNA (similar to cyanobacterial genomes), own 70S ribosomes (same as bacteria), binary fission reproduction, and phylogenetic analysis showing chloroplast DNA is most closely related to cyanobacteria
    [ ] Chloroplasts are artificial organelles created in laboratories — they do not exist naturally in any organism
    [ ] Chloroplasts are identical to mitochondria in structure and function — both perform photosynthesis and cellular respiration interchangeably
    Explanation: Chloroplasts and endosymbiotic theory: (1) Structure: double membrane (outer smooth, inner with invaginations), thylakoids (stacked into grana — site of light reactions, containing chlorophyll and photosystems I/II), stroma (fluid matrix — site of Calvin cycle/carbon fixation). Contain own circular DNA (~120-150 genes), 70S ribosomes, divide by binary fission. (2) Endosymbiotic theory (Lynn Margulis, 1967 paper 'On the Origin of Mitosing Cells'): ~1.5-2 billion years ago, ancestral heterotrophic eukaryote engulfed a photosynthetic cyanobacterium → endosymbiosis → over time, most genes transferred to host nucleus → cyanobacterium became chloroplast. Evidence: double membrane (inner = original cyanobacterial membrane, outer = host's phagocytic vacuole membrane), circular DNA (like bacteria), 70S ribosomes (bacterial-type, not 80S eukaryotic), binary fission (like bacteria), size similar to cyanobacteria (~1-10 μm), phylogenetics (chloroplast rRNA most closely related to cyanobacterial rRNA), sensitivity to antibiotics that target bacterial ribosomes. (3) Significance: photosynthesis by chloroplasts produces virtually all O₂ in Earth's atmosphere and is the foundation of most food chains.

  Q6. What are mitochondria, and what role do they play in cellular energy production?
    [ ] Mitochondria are single-membrane organelles that perform photosynthesis to produce glucose — they contain chlorophyll
    [*] Mitochondria are double-membrane organelles (outer membrane + highly folded inner membrane forming cristae) that are the primary site of aerobic cellular respiration — they convert chemical energy from glucose and other organic molecules into ATP through the citric acid cycle (in the matrix) and oxidative phosphorylation (electron transport chain + chemiosmosis on the inner membrane) — like chloroplasts, they have their own circular DNA and 70S ribosomes, supporting the endosymbiotic theory that they evolved from engulfed aerobic bacteria (~1.5-2 billion years ago, proposed by Lynn Margulis)
    [ ] Mitochondria are found only in plant cells, where they synthesize cellulose for the cell wall
    [ ] Mitochondria are the same organelle as lysosomes — both digest cellular waste using hydrolytic enzymes at acidic pH
    Explanation: Mitochondria — the powerhouse of the cell: (1) Structure: double membrane. Outer membrane (smooth, porin channels). Inner membrane (highly folded into cristae — increases surface area for ATP synthesis). Intermembrane space (between membranes — H⁺ ions accumulate here). Matrix (innermost compartment — contains mtDNA, 70S ribosomes, enzymes for citric acid cycle). (2) Function: aerobic respiration — glycolysis (cytoplasm) → pyruvate → matrix for citric acid cycle → NADH/FADH₂ → electron transport chain on inner membrane → chemiosmosis (ATP synthase uses H⁺ gradient to make ATP). ~30-32 ATP per glucose. (3) Endosymbiotic origin: originally free-living α-proteobacteria engulfed by ancestral eukaryote. Evidence: double membrane, circular DNA, 70S ribosomes, binary fission, phylogenetics. (4) Special features: maternal inheritance (mtDNA from mother only), high mutation rate, used in population genetics and forensics. (5) Mitochondrial diseases: mutations in mtDNA or nuclear genes for mitochondrial proteins → defective ATP production → especially affects high-energy-demand tissues (brain, heart, muscle). Examples: Leber's hereditary optic neuropathy, mitochondrial myopathy.

  Q7. What are the three components of the cytoskeleton, and how do they differ in structure and function?
    [ ] The cytoskeleton is a rigid, permanent structure made entirely of bone-like material — it never changes shape and has a single component
    [*] The three cytoskeletal components are: (1) Microtubules (largest, ~25 nm diameter, hollow tubes of α/β-tubulin — functions: cell shape, intracellular transport via kinesin/dynein motor proteins, mitotic spindle formation, structure of cilia and flagella with 9+2 arrangement); (2) Microfilaments/actin filaments (smallest, ~7 nm, two twisted actin chains — functions: cell shape, muscle contraction with myosin, cell movement via pseudopods, cytokinesis via contractile ring); (3) Intermediate filaments (~8-12 nm, fibrous proteins like keratin/vimentin/lamin — functions: mechanical strength, anchoring nucleus and organelles, resist tensile stress)
    [ ] The cytoskeleton consists only of cellulose microfibrils and is found exclusively in plant cells — animal cells lack internal structural support
    [ ] The cytoskeleton is made of RNA molecules that fold into 3D scaffolds inside the cell — it has no protein components
    Explanation: The cytoskeleton: a dynamic network of protein filaments. (1) Microtubules: largest (25 nm). Hollow tubes of α/β-tubulin heterodimers. Dynamic instability: constantly growing/shrinking from plus end. Functions: maintain cell shape, intracellular transport (kinesin = + end, dynein = − end), mitotic spindle (chromosome separation), cilia/flagella (9+2 axoneme arrangement — 9 doublet microtubules + 2 central singlets, dynein arms power bending). Colchicine/taxol: drugs that disrupt microtubules → used as anticancer agents. (2) Microfilaments (actin): smallest (7 nm). Two intertwined actin chains. Functions: cell shape (cortex beneath membrane), muscle contraction (actin-myosin interaction — sliding filament model), amoeboid movement (pseudopod extension), cytokinesis (cleavage furrow — contractile ring of actin/myosin). Cytochalasin: disrupts actin. (3) Intermediate filaments: medium (8-12 nm). Various proteins (keratins in epithelial cells, vimentin in connective tissue, neurofilaments in neurons, lamins in nuclear lamina). Most stable — rope-like tensile strength. Functions: mechanical support, nuclear anchoring, tissue-specific (e.g., epidermolysis bullosa from keratin mutations).

  Q8. What is the function of the large central vacuole in plant cells, and how does it compare to vacuoles in other cell types?
    [ ] The central vacuole produces ATP for the plant cell — it is the plant equivalent of mitochondria and is absent in all non-plant cells
    [*] The large central vacuole (tonoplast membrane) can occupy up to 80-90% of plant cell volume — it stores water, ions, nutrients, pigments (anthocyanins for flower color), and waste products — its water content generates turgor pressure against the cell wall, providing structural support and driving cell expansion during growth — other organisms also have vacuoles: food vacuoles (protists — phagocytosis/digestion), contractile vacuoles (freshwater protists — osmoregulation), and small storage vacuoles in animal cells
    [ ] The central vacuole is a solid structure made of cellulose that provides rigidity — it has no liquid contents and plays no role in storage
    [ ] Vacuoles are found only in bacterial cells where they serve as the nucleoid region for DNA storage
    Explanation: Vacuoles: membrane-bound storage compartments. (1) Plant central vacuole: large (up to 80-90% of cell volume), bounded by tonoplast membrane. Contents: water (turgor pressure), ions (K⁺, Cl⁻, Ca²⁺ for osmotic regulation), sugars, amino acids, organic acids (citric acid in citrus fruits), pigments (anthocyanins — red/blue/purple flower colors), defensive compounds (alkaloids, tannins — deter herbivores), waste products. Functions: turgor pressure (structural support in non-woody plants, drives cell expansion during growth), storage (nutrients, pigments, defensive chemicals), recycling (vacuolar enzymes break down proteins/organelles, similar to lysosomes), pH regulation (acidic vacuole pH maintained by H⁺ ATPase). (2) Food vacuoles: protists and phagocytic cells — formed by endocytosis → fuse with lysosomes → digest contents. (3) Contractile vacuoles: freshwater protists (Paramecium, Amoeba) — pump out excess water entering by osmosis → osmoregulation. (4) Animal cell vacuoles: smaller and more numerous — temporary storage.

  Q9. What are ribosomes, where are they found, and what is their function in protein synthesis?
    [ ] Ribosomes are membrane-bound organelles that produce lipids in the smooth endoplasmic reticulum — they have no role in protein synthesis
    [*] Ribosomes are non-membrane-bound molecular machines composed of ribosomal RNA (rRNA) and proteins that translate mRNA into polypeptide chains (protein synthesis) — eukaryotic ribosomes are 80S (60S + 40S subunits) while prokaryotic ribosomes are 70S (50S + 30S) — free ribosomes in the cytoplasm make proteins for use within the cell, while bound ribosomes on the rough ER make proteins destined for membranes, secretion, or lysosomes — rRNA is synthesized in the nucleolus, and ribosome subunits are assembled there before export through nuclear pores
    [ ] Ribosomes are found only in the nucleus and function exclusively in DNA replication during cell division
    [ ] Ribosomes are solid protein crystals that store amino acids until the cell needs them for energy production
    Explanation: Ribosomes — protein synthesis machines: (1) Composition: ~60% rRNA + ~40% protein. NOT membrane-bound (not organelles in the strict sense). (2) Size: eukaryotic = 80S (60S large subunit + 40S small subunit). Prokaryotic/mitochondrial/chloroplast = 70S (50S + 30S). 'S' = Svedberg sedimentation coefficient (not additive). (3) Assembly: rRNA genes transcribed in nucleolus → rRNA combined with ribosomal proteins (imported from cytoplasm) → subunits assembled → exported through nuclear pores → join in cytoplasm during translation initiation. (4) Locations: free ribosomes (cytoplasm — make cytoplasmic, nuclear, peroxisomal, mitochondrial proteins), bound ribosomes (attached to rough ER via signal sequence/SRP pathway — make membrane, secretory, and lysosomal proteins). Same ribosome can be free or bound depending on the mRNA being translated. (5) Clinical: difference between 70S and 80S ribosomes exploited by antibiotics (tetracycline, erythromycin, chloramphenicol, streptomycin target 70S → kill bacteria without harming human 80S ribosomes — selective toxicity).

  Q10. What are the key structural components and functions of the nucleus, and how does the nuclear envelope regulate molecular traffic?
    [ ] The nucleus is a solid structure without any pores or openings — molecules enter and exit by diffusing through the nuclear membrane like they cross the plasma membrane
    [*] The nucleus is bounded by a double-membrane nuclear envelope (continuous with the rough ER) perforated by ~3,000-4,000 nuclear pore complexes (NPCs, made of ~30 different nucleoporins) that regulate bidirectional molecular traffic — small molecules pass freely, but proteins >40 kDa require nuclear localization signals (NLS) for import and nuclear export signals (NES) for export, mediated by importins/exportins — the nucleus contains chromatin (DNA + histones), the nucleolus (rRNA synthesis and ribosome subunit assembly), and the nuclear lamina (intermediate filaments lining the inner membrane for structural support)
    [ ] The nucleus contains only proteins and has no DNA — genetic information is stored in the mitochondria instead
    [ ] The nuclear envelope is a single membrane identical to the plasma membrane — it has no special features for molecular transport
    Explanation: The nucleus — information center: (1) Nuclear envelope: double membrane, continuous with rough ER at places. Outer membrane: has ribosomes (like RER). Inner membrane: associated with nuclear lamina. (2) Nuclear pore complexes (NPCs): ~3,000-4,000 per nucleus, each ~125 MDa, made of ~30 nucleoporins. Central channel ~5.2 nm for passive diffusion (ions, small molecules). Active transport of large molecules: proteins with NLS (nuclear localization signal, e.g., PKKKRKV) → recognized by importin → transported through NPC using Ran-GTPase energy system. mRNA export: processed mRNA bound by export factors → exportin → NPC → cytoplasm. (3) Chromatin: DNA wrapped around histone octamers → nucleosomes (beads on a string). Euchromatin (loose, active transcription) vs. heterochromatin (condensed, inactive). During cell division: chromatin condenses → visible chromosomes. (4) Nucleolus: site of rRNA transcription (RNA Pol I) and ribosome subunit assembly. Can be 1 or more per nucleus. Largest during active growth. (5) Nuclear lamina: meshwork of intermediate filaments (lamins A, B, C) lining inner membrane. Supports nuclear shape, anchors chromatin, organizes nuclear pores. Lamin mutations → laminopathies (e.g., Hutchinson-Gilford progeria — premature aging).

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes the endoplasmic reticulum?
    [ ] A stack of flattened membrane sacs that modifies, sorts, and packages proteins for transport throughout the cell
    [*] An extensive network of membrane-enclosed tubules and sacs (cisternae) within the cytoplasm — the rough ER (studded with ribosomes) synthesizes, folds, and modifies proteins destined for membranes, secretion, or lysosomes, while the smooth ER (lacking ribosomes) synthesizes lipids, steroid hormones, detoxifies drugs and poisons, and stores calcium ions — the ER is continuous with the nuclear envelope and is the starting point of the secretory pathway
    [ ] A double-membrane organelle that produces ATP through chemiosmosis and oxidative phosphorylation during cellular respiration
    [ ] A membrane-bound vesicle containing hydrolytic enzymes that digest cellular waste at acidic pH
    Explanation: Endoplasmic reticulum (ER): an extensive membrane system continuous with the nuclear envelope. Two types: Rough ER (RER) — ribosomes on cytoplasmic surface. Synthesizes membrane and secretory proteins. Signal sequence → SRP → RER translocon → protein enters lumen. Folding (chaperones: BiP, calnexin), N-linked glycosylation, quality control (misfolded proteins → ERAD = ER-associated degradation → proteasome). Smooth ER (SER) — no ribosomes. Lipid synthesis (phospholipids, cholesterol → distributed to all membranes via vesicles and lipid transfer proteins). Steroid hormone synthesis (adrenal cortex, gonads). Drug detoxification (cytochrome P450 enzymes in liver). Ca²⁺ storage (sarcoplasmic reticulum in muscle). ER stress: accumulation of misfolded proteins → unfolded protein response (UPR) → if unresolved → apoptosis.

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes the Golgi apparatus?
    [ ] A network of membrane tubules where proteins are synthesized by ribosomes and initially folded
    [*] A stack of flattened membrane cisternae organized with a cis (receiving) face and a trans (shipping) face — it receives proteins and lipids from the endoplasmic reticulum, further modifies them (glycosylation, phosphorylation), sorts them by adding molecular 'address tags' (e.g., mannose-6-phosphate for lysosomal enzymes), and packages them into vesicles for delivery to their final destinations — discovered by Camillo Golgi (Nobel Prize 1906)
    [ ] A double-membrane organelle in plant cells where photosynthesis occurs, containing chlorophyll pigments
    [ ] A structure within the nucleus that produces ribosomal RNA and assembles ribosome subunits
    Explanation: Golgi apparatus: stack of 4-8 flattened membrane cisternae. Cis face (receiving, near ER) → medial → trans face (shipping, near plasma membrane). Functions: modification (O-linked glycosylation, trimming/modification of N-linked sugars from ER, phosphorylation — mannose-6-phosphate tag), sorting (address tags direct cargo), packaging (vesicles bud from trans-Golgi network to: plasma membrane, lysosomes, secretory granules). Vesicle coat proteins: COPII (ER → Golgi), COPI (Golgi → ER retrograde), clathrin (TGN → lysosomes/endosomes). Discovered by Camillo Golgi (1898) using his black reaction (silver nitrate staining of nerve tissue). Nobel Prize 1906.

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes a lysosome?
    [ ] An organelle that produces energy by breaking down glucose through the electron transport chain and oxidative phosphorylation
    [*] A membrane-bound organelle containing approximately 50 different hydrolytic enzymes (proteases, lipases, nucleases, glycosidases) that operate at an acidic pH (~4.5-5.0, maintained by proton pumps) — lysosomes digest materials from endocytosis, phagocytosis, and autophagy — discovered by Christian de Duve (Nobel Prize 1974) — deficiency in lysosomal enzymes causes lysosomal storage diseases such as Tay-Sachs and Gaucher disease
    [ ] A storage compartment found only in plant cells that contains water, pigments, and maintains turgor pressure
    [ ] A ribosome-studded membrane network where proteins are translated from mRNA and initially folded into their 3D conformations
    Explanation: Lysosome: the cell's digestive compartment. Membrane-bound, single membrane. ~50 acid hydrolases (optimal pH 4.5-5.0). pH maintained by V-type H⁺ ATPase (proton pump). Functions: heterophagy (digest extracellular material from endocytosis/phagocytosis), autophagy (recycle damaged organelles — Ohsumi, Nobel 2016), intracellular digestion. Formation: enzymes made on RER → Golgi adds M6P tag → sorted to lysosome. Safety: if lysosome ruptures, enzymes inactive at pH 7.2. Lysosomal storage diseases (~50): Tay-Sachs (hexosaminidase A ↓ → GM2 ganglioside accumulates in neurons → neurodegeneration), Gaucher (glucocerebrosidase ↓), Pompe (acid maltase ↓ → glycogen accumulates in muscle), Fabry (α-galactosidase A ↓). Treatment: enzyme replacement therapy (recombinant enzyme infusions).

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes a chloroplast?
    [ ] An organelle found in all eukaryotic cells that breaks down glucose to produce ATP through aerobic respiration
    [*] A double-membrane organelle found in plant and algal cells that is the site of photosynthesis — it contains an internal thylakoid membrane system (organized into stacked grana) where the light reactions capture solar energy, and a fluid stroma where the Calvin cycle fixes CO₂ into organic molecules — chloroplasts contain chlorophyll pigments, their own circular DNA, and 70S ribosomes, supporting the endosymbiotic theory that they evolved from engulfed cyanobacteria (Lynn Margulis, 1967)
    [ ] A membrane-enclosed sac containing digestive enzymes that operate at pH 4.5 to break down cellular waste
    [ ] A fibrous protein network that maintains cell shape, powers cell movement, and separates chromosomes during cell division
    Explanation: Chloroplast: site of photosynthesis in plants and algae. Structure: outer membrane (permeable to small molecules), inner membrane (selective transport), thylakoid membrane system (grana — stacks of disc-shaped thylakoids — connected by stroma lamellae), stroma (fluid matrix). Photosynthesis: light reactions (thylakoid membranes — photosystem II → water splitting/O₂ release → electron transport → photosystem I → NADPH + chemiosmosis → ATP), Calvin cycle (stroma — CO₂ fixation by RuBisCO → G3P → glucose). Pigments: chlorophyll a (P680, P700), chlorophyll b, carotenoids (accessory pigments, photoprotection). Endosymbiotic origin: from cyanobacteria (~1.5-2 BYA). Evidence: double membrane, circular DNA (~120-150 genes), 70S ribosomes, binary fission, phylogenetics. Significance: produces virtually all O₂ in Earth's atmosphere, foundation of most food chains, absorbs CO₂ (carbon sink).

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes a vacuole?
    [ ] A protein synthesis complex composed of rRNA and ribosomal proteins that translates mRNA into polypeptide chains
    [*] A membrane-bound storage compartment — the large central vacuole in plant cells (bounded by the tonoplast membrane, occupying up to 80-90% of cell volume) stores water, ions, nutrients, pigments, and waste while generating turgor pressure essential for structural support — other types include food vacuoles (endocytic digestion in protists), contractile vacuoles (osmoregulation in freshwater protists), and smaller storage vacuoles in animal cells
    [ ] A structure made of tubulin proteins that forms the mitotic spindle and separates chromosomes during cell division
    [ ] The outer protective layer of a plant cell composed of cellulose microfibrils that provides rigidity and prevents excessive water uptake
    Explanation: Vacuole: membrane-bound storage compartment. Plant central vacuole: bounded by tonoplast membrane. Occupies up to 80-90% of mature plant cell volume. Stores: water (turgor pressure), ions (K⁺, Cl⁻, Ca²⁺), sugars, amino acids, organic acids, pigments (anthocyanins), defensive compounds (alkaloids, tannins), waste products. Functions: turgor pressure (structural support → wilting = turgor loss; cell expansion during growth), storage, recycling (contains hydrolases similar to lysosomes), pH regulation. Other vacuoles: food vacuoles (protists/phagocytes — endocytic digestion), contractile vacuoles (freshwater protists — Paramecium — osmoregulation), gas vacuoles (some aquatic bacteria — buoyancy), smaller storage vacuoles in animal cells.

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes the endomembrane system?
    [ ] A collection of organelles that all produce ATP, including mitochondria, chloroplasts, and peroxisomes
    [*] A network of interconnected membrane-bound organelles — including the nuclear envelope, endoplasmic reticulum, Golgi apparatus, lysosomes, vesicles, and the plasma membrane — that work together through vesicle trafficking to synthesize, modify, sort, and deliver proteins and lipids throughout the cell and to the cell surface
    [ ] The collection of all DNA-containing organelles in the cell, specifically the nucleus, mitochondria, and chloroplasts
    [ ] A protein fiber network consisting of microtubules, microfilaments, and intermediate filaments that maintains cell shape
    Explanation: Endomembrane system: organelles connected by direct physical continuity or vesicle transfer. Components: nuclear envelope (continuous with RER), rough ER (protein synthesis), smooth ER (lipid synthesis, detox), Golgi apparatus (modification, sorting, packaging), lysosomes (digestion), vesicles (transport), plasma membrane (exocytosis/endocytosis). The secretory pathway: nucleus → RER → COPII vesicles → Golgi cis → trans-Golgi network → clathrin/secretory vesicles → destination. NOT included: mitochondria and chloroplasts (endosymbiotic origin, own DNA/ribosomes), peroxisomes (may derive from ER but functionally independent). Nobel recognition: Palade (1974 — secretory pathway), Rothman/Schekman/Südhof (2013 — vesicle trafficking mechanisms).

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes the cytoskeleton?
    [ ] A rigid external shell made of calcium carbonate that protects the cell from physical damage, similar to the exoskeleton of arthropods
    [*] A dynamic network of protein filaments in the cytoplasm — consisting of microtubules (25 nm, tubulin — cell shape, transport, spindle, cilia/flagella), microfilaments (7 nm, actin — cell shape, movement, cytokinesis, muscle contraction), and intermediate filaments (8-12 nm, various proteins like keratin — mechanical strength, tensile resistance) — the cytoskeleton maintains cell shape, enables cell movement, drives intracellular transport, and powers cell division
    [ ] A membrane-enclosed system of tubules where proteins are synthesized and lipids are manufactured in the cytoplasm
    [ ] A layer of cellulose fibers found only in plant cells that provides rigid structural support outside the plasma membrane
    Explanation: Cytoskeleton: dynamic protein network providing structure, movement, and organization. Three components: (1) Microtubules (25 nm) — α/β-tubulin polymers. Dynamic instability (grow/shrink rapidly). Functions: cell shape, intracellular transport (kinesin toward + end, dynein toward − end), mitotic spindle (chromosome separation), cilia/flagella (9+2 axoneme). Drug targets: colchicine, taxol (paclitaxel), vincristine → anticancer (disrupt mitotic spindle). (2) Microfilaments/actin (7 nm) — two twisted F-actin chains. Functions: cortical network (cell shape, membrane support), muscle contraction (actin-myosin sliding filament), amoeboid movement (pseudopods), cytokinesis (contractile ring). Drug: cytochalasin. (3) Intermediate filaments (8-12 nm) — various proteins (keratins, vimentin, neurofilaments, lamins). Most stable, rope-like. Functions: mechanical strength, anchoring organelles/nucleus. Mutations: epidermolysis bullosa simplex (keratin), laminopathies (lamin A/C).

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes a mitochondrion?
    [ ] An organelle in plant cells where photosynthesis captures light energy and converts it into glucose and oxygen
    [*] A double-membrane organelle that is the primary site of aerobic cellular respiration — the inner membrane (folded into cristae to increase surface area) houses the electron transport chain and ATP synthase, while the matrix contains enzymes for the citric acid cycle — mitochondria have their own circular DNA and 70S ribosomes, supporting the endosymbiotic theory that they evolved from engulfed α-proteobacteria approximately 1.5-2 billion years ago
    [ ] A single-membrane organelle containing hydrolytic enzymes that digest waste materials at an acidic pH of 4.5
    [ ] A non-membrane-bound complex of rRNA and proteins that translates mRNA into polypeptide chains in the cytoplasm
    Explanation: Mitochondrion: the 'powerhouse of the cell.' Double membrane: outer (porin channels, permeable to small molecules), inner (cristae — extensive folds, houses ETC complexes I-IV + ATP synthase, impermeable to most molecules → creates H⁺ gradient for chemiosmosis). Matrix: citric acid cycle enzymes, mtDNA, 70S ribosomes, beta-oxidation of fatty acids. ATP production: glycolysis (cytoplasm, 2 ATP) → pyruvate oxidation → citric acid cycle (matrix, 2 ATP + NADH/FADH₂) → ETC/oxidative phosphorylation (inner membrane, ~26-28 ATP) = ~30-32 ATP total per glucose. Endosymbiotic origin: from α-proteobacteria (~1.5-2 BYA). Maternal inheritance (mtDNA). Mitochondrial diseases affect high-energy tissues (brain, heart, muscle). Also involved in: apoptosis (cytochrome c release), calcium homeostasis, iron-sulfur cluster synthesis.

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes the nucleolus?
    [ ] A membrane-bound organelle in the cytoplasm that sorts and packages proteins into vesicles for transport
    [*] A dense, non-membrane-bound structure within the nucleus where ribosomal RNA (rRNA) is synthesized by RNA polymerase I and where ribosome subunits are partially assembled before being exported through nuclear pores to the cytoplasm — the nucleolus forms around nucleolar organizer regions (NORs) on specific chromosomes and is prominent in cells with high rates of protein synthesis
    [ ] The region of the cytoplasm between the plasma membrane and the nuclear envelope, containing all organelles and the cytosol
    [ ] A whip-like projection from the cell surface made of microtubules in a 9+2 arrangement used for cell locomotion
    Explanation: Nucleolus: dense region within the nucleus (not membrane-bound). Function: rRNA synthesis (RNA Pol I transcribes 45S pre-rRNA → processed into 28S, 18S, 5.8S rRNA) and ribosome subunit assembly. rRNA genes (rDNA): located at nucleolar organizer regions (NORs) on chromosomes 13, 14, 15, 21, 22 in humans (~400 copies — multiple copies to produce massive rRNA amounts). Assembly: rRNA + ribosomal proteins (made in cytoplasm, imported) → partial assembly of 60S and 40S subunits → exported through nuclear pores → final assembly in cytoplasm during translation initiation. Size: prominent in cells with high protein synthesis rates (secretory cells, cancer cells — nucleolar size correlates with growth rate). Can have 1 or more nucleoli per nucleus. Disappears during mitosis (chromatin condensation) and reforms after cell division.

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes the extracellular matrix (ECM)?
    [ ] The fluid inside the cell between the plasma membrane and the nuclear envelope — it contains all organelles and dissolved molecules
    [*] A complex network of proteins and carbohydrates secreted by animal cells that lies outside the plasma membrane — major components include collagen (structural strength), proteoglycans (hydration and compression resistance), fibronectin and laminin (connect ECM to cell surface integrins) — the ECM provides structural support, regulates cell behavior (growth, differentiation, migration), facilitates cell-to-cell signaling, and is essential for tissue integrity
    [ ] The rigid cell wall made of cellulose found exclusively in plant cells that prevents over-expansion in hypotonic solutions
    [ ] A mesh of intermediate filament proteins lining the inner surface of the nuclear envelope that provides structural support to the nucleus
    Explanation: Extracellular matrix (ECM): the structural and signaling network outside animal cells. Components: (1) Collagen: most abundant protein in the body (~30% of total protein). Triple helix of three α chains. Types: Type I (bone, skin, tendon — tensile strength), Type II (cartilage), Type IV (basement membranes). (2) Proteoglycans: core protein + glycosaminoglycans (GAGs: hyaluronic acid, chondroitin sulfate, heparan sulfate). Highly hydrated → resist compressive forces (cartilage). (3) Fibronectin: connects ECM to cells via integrins (transmembrane receptors). (4) Laminin: major component of basement membranes. Functions: structural support, cell adhesion via integrins (outside-in and inside-out signaling), regulation of cell growth/differentiation/migration/survival, wound healing, tissue development. ECM remodeling: matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) degrade ECM — important in development, wound healing, and (pathologically) in cancer metastasis. Plant equivalent: cell wall (cellulose microfibrils, hemicellulose, pectin, lignin).

  Q21. A patient is diagnosed with Tay-Sachs disease, a fatal genetic disorder in which the lysosomal enzyme hexosaminidase A is deficient. The patient's neurons accumulate massive amounts of GM2 ganglioside, leading to progressive neurological deterioration. Explain how a defective lysosomal enzyme causes this disease, and why neurons are particularly affected.
    [ ] Hexosaminidase A is a mitochondrial enzyme — its deficiency prevents ATP production specifically in neurons, causing energy failure and cell death
    [*] Normally, lysosomes use hexosaminidase A to break down GM2 ganglioside (a glycolipid component of neuronal membranes) — without this enzyme, GM2 accumulates in lysosomes → lysosomes swell enormously (up to 10× normal size) → disrupts normal cellular function → triggers apoptosis — neurons are particularly vulnerable because they have the highest turnover of gangliosides (enriched in brain tissue) and are post-mitotic (cannot divide to dilute accumulating substrate) — this is a lysosomal storage disease demonstrating that lysosomes are essential for ongoing cellular recycling
    [ ] Tay-Sachs is caused by a defective ribosome that cannot synthesize gangliosides — the accumulation occurs because the cell overproduces lipids in the smooth ER
    [ ] GM2 ganglioside accumulates in the Golgi apparatus because without hexosaminidase A, the Golgi cannot package proteins correctly — lysosomes are unaffected
    Explanation: Tay-Sachs disease — lysosomal storage disease: (1) Cause: autosomal recessive mutations in HEXA gene → deficient hexosaminidase A enzyme → cannot cleave GM2 ganglioside in lysosomes. (2) Pathology: GM2 ganglioside is a normal glycolipid component of neuronal membranes. Neurons continuously internalize and recycle membrane lipids → gangliosides delivered to lysosomes for degradation. Without hexosaminidase A → GM2 accumulates → lysosomes swell (membranous cytoplasmic bodies) → disrupts axonal transport, synaptic function, and triggers inflammation/apoptosis. (3) Why neurons? Highest ganglioside content of any cell type. Post-mitotic — cannot divide to dilute substrate. High metabolic rate. Long axons vulnerable to transport disruption. (4) Clinical: infantile form (most severe) — normal at birth → developmental regression at 3-6 months → seizures, blindness (cherry-red spot on retina), death by age 3-5. (5) Prevalence: Ashkenazi Jewish population (~1/30 carrier frequency). (6) This demonstrates that lysosomal function is not just 'garbage disposal' — it's essential for continuous cellular homeostasis. ~50 known lysosomal storage diseases collectively affect ~1/7,700 births.

  Q22. A pharmaceutical company is developing a cancer drug that targets microtubules. They know that taxol (paclitaxel) from Pacific yew trees and vincristine from periwinkle plants both disrupt microtubule dynamics but in opposite ways. How does each drug affect microtubules, and why does disrupting microtubules kill cancer cells?
    [ ] Both drugs dissolve the plasma membrane of cancer cells — microtubules are irrelevant to cancer cell death
    [*] Taxol stabilizes microtubules (prevents depolymerization → microtubules cannot shorten), while vincristine destabilizes microtubules (prevents polymerization → microtubules cannot grow) — both approaches prevent the dynamic assembly/disassembly of the mitotic spindle that is essential for chromosome separation during cell division → cells are arrested in mitosis → cannot complete cell division → apoptosis — rapidly dividing cancer cells are particularly vulnerable because they undergo frequent mitosis
    [ ] Both drugs enhance microtubule assembly, making the mitotic spindle extra strong and causing cells to divide too rapidly → cell exhaustion → death
    [ ] These drugs target intermediate filaments, not microtubules — they destroy the structural framework of the cell, causing it to collapse
    Explanation: Microtubule-targeting anticancer drugs: (1) Normal mitosis: microtubules of the mitotic spindle undergo rapid assembly (polymerization) and disassembly (depolymerization) — this dynamic instability is essential for: capturing chromosomes at kinetochores, aligning chromosomes at metaphase plate, separating sister chromatids during anaphase. (2) Taxol (paclitaxel, from Taxus brevifolia): binds β-tubulin → stabilizes microtubules → prevents depolymerization → 'frozen' spindle cannot function dynamically → mitotic arrest at metaphase → apoptosis. Used for: breast, ovarian, lung cancers. (3) Vincristine/vinblastine (from Catharanthus roseus): bind tubulin dimers → prevent polymerization → microtubules cannot assemble → no functional spindle → mitotic arrest → apoptosis. Used for: lymphomas, leukemias. (4) Both exploit the same vulnerability: dividing cells require dynamic microtubules. Cancer cells divide more frequently → more opportunities to encounter the drug during mitosis. (5) Side effects: affect all dividing cells (hair loss, bone marrow suppression, GI damage — because these tissues have rapidly dividing cells that also need microtubules for mitosis). Neuropathy (neurons depend on microtubule-based axonal transport).

  Q23. A researcher uses cell fractionation and differential centrifugation to isolate organelles from liver cells. She subjects a cell homogenate to successive centrifugation spins at increasing speeds: 1,000g, 10,000g, 100,000g, and then ultracentrifugation of the final supernatant. What organelles are collected at each step, and what principle determines the separation?
    [ ] All organelles pellet at the same speed regardless of their size — centrifugation cannot separate different organelles because they all have the same density
    [*] Organelles separate based on size and density — larger/denser organelles sediment at lower speeds: 1,000g pellets nuclei and cell debris (largest); 10,000g pellets mitochondria, chloroplasts, lysosomes, and peroxisomes; 100,000g pellets microsomes (ER and Golgi membrane fragments) and plasma membrane vesicles; ultracentrifugation of supernatant yields ribosomes and large macromolecular complexes — this technique was pioneered by Albert Claude and Christian de Duve (Nobel Prize 1974) and was essential for discovering the function of each organelle
    [ ] Centrifugation separates organelles by their color — green organelles (chloroplasts) pellet first, followed by brown (mitochondria), then clear (ER)
    [ ] Only the first spin at 1,000g is useful — all subsequent spins produce identical pellets containing mixed organelles
    Explanation: Cell fractionation and differential centrifugation: (1) Principle: centrifugal force causes particles to sediment based on their sedimentation coefficient, which depends on size, shape, and density. Larger/denser organelles sediment at lower centrifugal forces. (2) Procedure: homogenize cells → sequential centrifugation at increasing speeds. (a) 1,000g (10 min): nuclei, unbroken cells, cell debris (P1 pellet). (b) 10,000g (20 min): mitochondria, chloroplasts (plant cells), lysosomes, peroxisomes (P2 pellet). (c) 100,000g (60 min): microsomes (rough ER and smooth ER membrane fragments), Golgi vesicles, plasma membrane fragments (P3 pellet). (d) Ultracentrifugation (>100,000g, 2-3 hrs): ribosomes, large macromolecular complexes (P4 pellet). Supernatant S4: cytosol (soluble proteins, small molecules). (3) Further purification: density gradient centrifugation (sucrose or CsCl gradients) for higher resolution separation. (4) Historical significance: Albert Claude (developed cell fractionation, 1930s-40s), Christian de Duve (discovered lysosomes and peroxisomes using this technique), George Palade (used EM + fractionation to elucidate secretory pathway) — all shared Nobel Prize in Physiology/Medicine, 1974.

  Q24. A child inherits a mutation in a gene for a nuclear pore complex protein (nucleoporin). As a result, nuclear transport of proteins and mRNA export are severely impaired. Predict the consequences for cellular function.
    [ ] Nuclear pore mutations would have no effect because molecules can freely diffuse through the nuclear envelope's lipid bilayer without any pore structures
    [*] Nuclear transport defects would be catastrophic: (1) proteins with NLS (nuclear localization signals) — like transcription factors, histones, DNA repair enzymes — cannot enter the nucleus → gene expression and DNA maintenance fail; (2) mRNA cannot be exported through defective NPCs → no mRNA reaches ribosomes in cytoplasm → protein synthesis collapses; (3) ribosome subunits assembled in the nucleolus cannot be exported → ribosome shortage compounds protein synthesis failure — essentially, the nucleus becomes isolated from the cytoplasm, disrupting the central dogma at multiple points
    [ ] Only lipid synthesis in the smooth ER would be affected — nuclear pores regulate lipid transport exclusively, not protein or RNA movement
    [ ] The mutation would only affect the cell during division by preventing chromosome condensation — interphase functions would remain completely normal
    Explanation: Nuclear pore complex (NPC) dysfunction: (1) Normal NPC function: ~3,000-4,000 NPCs per nucleus. Each NPC: ~30 nucleoporins, central channel for passive diffusion of small molecules (<40 kDa), active transport for larger cargoes. Import: proteins with NLS → recognized by importins → translocation through NPC → Ran-GTP releases cargo. Export: mRNA bound to export factors → exportins → NPC → cytoplasm; ribosome subunits similarly exported. (2) Consequences of NPC dysfunction: (a) Import failure: transcription factors stuck in cytoplasm → gene expression disrupted. Histones not imported → chromatin structure collapses. DNA repair proteins absent → genomic instability. RNA polymerases not imported → transcription stops. (b) Export failure: mRNA trapped in nucleus → no new protein synthesis. Pre-ribosomal subunits trapped → ribosome depletion → accelerates protein synthesis collapse. tRNA not exported → translation stops. (c) Combined: complete breakdown of information flow between nucleus and cytoplasm. (3) Clinical: some nucleoporin mutations are embryonic lethal. Others linked to: primary biliary cholangitis (autoimmune — anti-nucleoporin antibodies), cancer (altered nuclear transport in tumor cells), and neurodegenerative diseases (nuclear pore deterioration with aging → protein aggregation).

  Q25. Scientists studying I-cell disease (mucolipidosis II) discover that patients' cells secrete lysosomal enzymes into the extracellular space instead of targeting them to lysosomes. The defect is traced to the inability to add the mannose-6-phosphate (M6P) tag in the Golgi apparatus. How does this explain the disease, and what does it reveal about the endomembrane system's sorting mechanism?
    [ ] Mannose-6-phosphate is a nutrient that lysosomes need to function — without it, lysosomal enzymes work normally but run out of energy
    [*] Normally, the Golgi adds mannose-6-phosphate (M6P) tags to lysosomal enzymes → M6P receptors in the trans-Golgi network recognize the tag and sort these enzymes into clathrin-coated vesicles destined for lysosomes — in I-cell disease, the enzyme GlcNAc phosphotransferase is defective → cannot add M6P tags → lysosomal enzymes follow the default secretory pathway → secreted out of the cell instead of being delivered to lysosomes → lysosomes lack digestive enzymes → undigested material accumulates → dense inclusion bodies visible in cells (hence 'inclusion cell' disease) — this demonstrates that the Golgi actively sorts proteins using molecular address tags, and the default pathway without a tag is secretion
    [ ] I-cell disease occurs because the rough ER fails to synthesize any proteins — the M6P tag is irrelevant to lysosomal enzyme targeting
    [ ] Lysosomal enzymes are targeted to lysosomes by their amino acid sequence alone — the Golgi and M6P system play no role in sorting
    Explanation: I-cell disease (mucolipidosis II) — endomembrane sorting revealed: (1) Normal pathway: lysosomal enzymes synthesized on RER → enter ER lumen → transport to Golgi → cis-Golgi: GlcNAc phosphotransferase recognizes a signal patch on lysosomal enzyme surface → adds N-acetylglucosamine-1-phosphate → phosphodiesterase removes GlcNAc → exposes mannose-6-phosphate (M6P) tag → trans-Golgi network: M6P receptors bind tagged enzymes → clathrin-coated vesicles bud off → delivered to late endosomes → mature into lysosomes → acidic pH releases enzymes from M6P receptors → receptors recycled. (2) I-cell disease: GlcNAc phosphotransferase defective → no M6P tag → enzymes not recognized by M6P receptors → follow default secretory pathway → secreted extracellularly → lysosomes empty → undigested substrates accumulate (inclusion bodies). (3) Key insight: the Golgi uses molecular address tags (M6P) for sorting. Without the tag, proteins go to the default destination (secretion). This proves: (a) the endomembrane system actively sorts cargo, (b) sorting requires specific molecular recognition, (c) the default pathway is constitutive secretion. (4) Clinical: severe developmental delays, skeletal abnormalities, coarse facial features. Usually fatal by age 5-8.

  Q26. A forensic scientist examines cells from an unknown tissue sample under an electron microscope. She observes abundant rough ER, a prominent Golgi apparatus with many transport vesicles, and numerous secretory granules near the plasma membrane. What type of cell is this most likely, and how do these organelle features relate to the cell's function?
    [ ] This is a red blood cell — the abundant ER produces hemoglobin, and the Golgi packages it for transport in the bloodstream
    [*] This is most likely a secretory cell (e.g., pancreatic acinar cell secreting digestive enzymes, or plasma cell secreting antibodies) — the abundant rough ER produces large quantities of secretory proteins → prominent Golgi modifies, sorts, and packages them into secretory granules → granules accumulate near the plasma membrane awaiting a signal → regulated exocytosis releases contents extracellularly — the organelle profile directly reflects the cell's primary function of protein secretion, demonstrating the structure-function relationship 
    [ ] This is a fat (adipose) cell — the rough ER stores lipid droplets, the Golgi converts glucose into fat, and the vesicles contain stored triglycerides
    [ ] This is a muscle cell — the ER contracts to produce movement, the Golgi stores calcium ions, and the vesicles contain actin and myosin
    Explanation: Organelle profile reveals cell function: (1) Abundant RER + prominent Golgi + secretory granules = specialized secretory cell. The endomembrane system is highly developed to support massive protein production and export. (2) Example cells: Pancreatic acinar cells: secrete digestive enzymes (trypsinogen, amylase, lipase) → regulated exocytosis triggered by cholecystokinin (CCK). Plasma cells (differentiated B lymphocytes): secrete ~2,000 antibody molecules per second → extensive RER fills most of cytoplasm. Goblet cells: secrete mucus (glycoproteins). (3) Structure-function principle: cells with different functions have different organelle profiles. Secretory cells: abundant RER + Golgi. Steroid-secreting cells (adrenal cortex): abundant SER (steroid synthesis). Muscle cells: abundant mitochondria (ATP for contraction) + extensive SER (sarcoplasmic reticulum for Ca²⁺ storage). Phagocytic cells: abundant lysosomes. (4) EM observation: George Palade's Nobel Prize-winning work (1974) used EM to trace the secretory pathway through RER → Golgi → secretory vesicles → exocytosis in pancreatic acinar cells.

  Q27. Lynn Margulis proposed the endosymbiotic theory in 1967, initially facing strong skepticism from the scientific community. Now it is widely accepted. What specific evidence for the endosymbiotic origin of mitochondria and chloroplasts makes this theory so compelling?
    [ ] The only evidence is that mitochondria and chloroplasts are round — many structures are round, so this alone proves nothing about their origin
    [*] Multiple independent lines of evidence converge: (1) double membranes (inner = original bacterial membrane, outer = host's phagocytic vacuole); (2) own circular DNA (similar to bacterial chromosomes, not linear eukaryotic DNA); (3) own 70S ribosomes (bacterial-type, not 80S eukaryotic — sensitive to bacterial antibiotics like chloramphenicol); (4) binary fission reproduction (like bacteria, independent of cell cycle); (5) size similar to bacteria (~1-10 μm); (6) phylogenetic analysis — mitochondrial rRNA most closely related to α-proteobacteria; chloroplast rRNA most closely related to cyanobacteria
    [ ] The theory is accepted purely because Lynn Margulis was a famous scientist — there is no experimental evidence, only her authority as a researcher
    [ ] The only evidence is that both organelles produce ATP — since bacteria also produce ATP, they must be related, and no other evidence exists
    Explanation: Endosymbiotic theory — evidence compilation: (1) Double membranes: inner membrane = original endosymbiont's plasma membrane (has bacterial-type lipid composition); outer membrane = host cell's phagocytic vacuole membrane. (2) Circular DNA: both organelles have small circular chromosomes, like bacteria. mtDNA: ~16,569 bp in humans (37 genes). Chloroplast DNA: ~120,000-150,000 bp (~120 genes). Both lack histones (most — some exceptions). (3) 70S ribosomes: same size and composition as bacterial ribosomes. Sensitive to antibiotics that target bacterial ribosomes (chloramphenicol, erythromycin, tetracycline) but resistant to cycloheximide (which inhibits 80S eukaryotic ribosomes). (4) Binary fission: divide independently of the cell's nuclear division. Constriction of inner membrane (like bacteria) rather than mitosis. (5) Phylogenetics: 16S rRNA sequencing places mitochondria in α-Proteobacteria clade (closest living relatives: Rickettsia) and chloroplasts in cyanobacteria clade. (6) Gene transfer: many ancestral endosymbiont genes have been transferred to the host nucleus over evolutionary time → organelles now depend on nuclear-encoded proteins (imported via transit peptides through TOM/TIM complexes for mitochondria, TOC/TIC for chloroplasts). (7) Present-day analogs: Paulinella chromatophora has a 'chromatophore' (photosynthetic organelle) acquired from cyanobacteria ~60 MYA — endosymbiosis in progress.

  Q28. A patient with Hutchinson-Gilford progeria syndrome (premature aging) has a mutation in the LMNA gene, which encodes lamin A — a key component of the nuclear lamina (intermediate filament meshwork lining the inner nuclear membrane). How does a defective nuclear structural protein lead to accelerated aging?
    [ ] Lamin A mutations cause the endoplasmic reticulum to produce toxic proteins — the nuclear lamina is irrelevant to aging
    [*] The LMNA mutation produces an abnormal protein called progerin that cannot be properly processed → progerin accumulates in the nuclear lamina → distorts nuclear shape (lobulated, blebbed nuclei) → disrupts chromatin organization (heterochromatin loss) → impairs DNA repair (repair proteins cannot localize correctly) → alters gene expression patterns → cellular senescence — this demonstrates that the nuclear lamina is not just structural support but is essential for genome organization, DNA repair, gene regulation, and overall cellular health — interestingly, normal aging also involves progerin accumulation at low levels
    [ ] The mutation only affects red blood cells — since red blood cells lack a nucleus, this has no effect on other cell types or aging
    [ ] Lamin A is a mitochondrial protein that produces ATP — without it, cells lack energy and age faster due to metabolic failure
    Explanation: Hutchinson-Gilford progeria and the nuclear lamina: (1) LMNA gene: encodes lamin A and lamin C (alternative splicing). Lamin A: type V intermediate filament, major component of nuclear lamina (meshwork lining inner nuclear membrane). (2) Progeria mutation: G608G (GGC→GGT, silent at amino acid level but creates cryptic splice site) → abnormal mRNA → truncated lamin A called 'progerin' → lacks 50 amino acids near C-terminus → cannot undergo final post-translational processing (farnesyl group permanently attached) → progerin inserts into nuclear lamina but cannot be properly removed → accumulates → disrupts lamina structure. (3) Consequences: nuclear shape distortion (lobulated, blebbed nuclei — visible by EM), heterochromatin loss (chromatin detaches from lamina → gene expression dysregulation), DNA repair impairment (repair complexes require proper nuclear organization to localize at damage sites → genomic instability), telomere dysfunction, premature senescence. (4) Clinical: children appear normal at birth → by age 1-2: growth failure, hair loss, thin skin, cardiovascular disease → death by average age 13 (heart attack/stroke from accelerated atherosclerosis). (5) Connection to normal aging: all humans accumulate small amounts of progerin with age → normal aging may involve the same mechanisms but at much slower rates. (6) Treatment: lonafarnib (farnesyltransferase inhibitor) — prevents progerin from attaching to nuclear membrane → extends lifespan by ~2.5 years (FDA approved 2020).

  Q29. A liver hepatocyte (liver cell) has an unusually extensive smooth endoplasmic reticulum compared to most other cell types. When a patient chronically consumes alcohol or takes barbiturates, the liver hepatocytes' smooth ER proliferates even further. Explain why liver cells have extensive SER and why it proliferates in response to these substances.
    [ ] The liver SER stores water to dilute alcohol — it proliferates to hold more water and has no enzymatic function in drug metabolism
    [*] Liver hepatocyte SER is extensive because it is the primary site of drug and toxin detoxification — smooth ER contains cytochrome P450 enzymes (a family of ~57 enzymes in humans) that catalyze oxidation reactions to make hydrophobic toxins, drugs, and alcohol more water-soluble for excretion by the kidneys — chronic exposure to alcohol or barbiturates induces SER proliferation (the cell produces more detoxification capacity) → explains drug tolerance (higher enzyme levels metabolize drugs faster → need larger doses) and drug interactions (increased P450 activity can accelerate metabolism of other drugs)
    [ ] Liver cells use the smooth ER for protein synthesis — it proliferates because alcohol and barbiturates increase the demand for new liver proteins
    [ ] The observed SER proliferation is an artifact of alcohol-induced liver cell swelling — no actual increase in SER membranes occurs
    Explanation: Liver smooth ER and detoxification: (1) Liver hepatocyte SER functions: drug/toxin detoxification (cytochrome P450 enzymes — Phase I metabolism: oxidation, reduction, hydrolysis → add/expose polar groups → make hydrophobic compounds more water-soluble), lipid/cholesterol synthesis, bile acid production, glycogen metabolism (glucose-6-phosphatase in SER membrane converts G6P → glucose for release into blood), bilirubin conjugation. (2) Cytochrome P450 (CYP) enzymes: ~57 human P450 genes. Major drug-metabolizing: CYP3A4 (metabolizes ~50% of all drugs), CYP2D6, CYP2C9. Alcohol dehydrogenase (cytoplasm) + CYP2E1 (SER) metabolize ethanol. (3) Chronic alcohol/drug exposure: induces CYP enzyme expression → SER membrane proliferation (visible by electron microscopy — SER area can double or triple). Consequences: drug tolerance (faster metabolism → need higher doses), cross-tolerance (other drugs metabolized faster too), dangerous drug interactions. (4) Cirrhosis: chronic alcohol → repeated liver injury → fibrosis → cirrhosis → liver failure. (5) Grapefruit juice: contains furanocoumarins that inhibit CYP3A4 in intestinal cells → reduces first-pass metabolism of certain drugs → increases drug levels in blood → potential toxicity. Example of organelle function directly affecting pharmacology.

  Q30. The 2016 Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine was awarded to Yoshinori Ohsumi for his work on autophagy — the process by which cells 'eat themselves' by enclosing damaged organelles in double-membrane vesicles (autophagosomes) that then fuse with lysosomes for digestion and recycling. Why is autophagy essential for cellular health, and what happens when it malfunctions?
    [ ] Autophagy is harmful to cells — it destroys healthy organelles and the Nobel Prize was for discovering how to stop this destructive process
    [*] Autophagy is an essential quality control mechanism that removes damaged organelles (e.g., dysfunctional mitochondria → mitophagy), misfolded protein aggregates, and intracellular pathogens — it also provides nutrients during starvation by recycling cellular components into amino acids and fatty acids — when autophagy malfunctions: damaged mitochondria accumulate → increased reactive oxygen species (ROS) → DNA damage → cancer risk increases; protein aggregates accumulate → neurodegenerative diseases (Alzheimer's, Parkinson's, Huntington's); intracellular pathogens escape destruction → increased infection susceptibility
    [ ] Autophagy only occurs in plant cells and has no role in human health — the Nobel Prize was awarded for work in yeast that has no medical applications
    [ ] Autophagy is the same process as apoptosis (programmed cell death) — both destroy the entire cell, and there is no difference between them
    Explanation: Autophagy — Nobel Prize 2016 (Yoshinori Ohsumi): (1) Process: isolation membrane (phagophore) forms → elongates around target → closes to form autophagosome (double-membrane vesicle) → autophagosome fuses with lysosome → autolysosome → lysosomal enzymes digest contents → building blocks (amino acids, fatty acids, nucleotides) recycled to cytoplasm. Key proteins: ATG (autophagy-related) family — Ohsumi identified 15 essential ATG genes in yeast. (2) Types: macroautophagy (bulk — entire organelles), selective autophagy: mitophagy (damaged mitochondria), pexophagy (peroxisomes), ribophagy (ribosomes), aggrephagy (protein aggregates), xenophagy (intracellular pathogens). (3) Functions: quality control (remove damaged organelles, prevent ROS accumulation), nutrient recycling (during starvation — sustains cell until nutrients available), development (cellular remodeling), immunity (xenophagy destroys intracellular bacteria like M. tuberculosis). (4) Disease links: defective autophagy → neurodegenerative diseases (protein aggregates: Aβ/tau in Alzheimer's, α-synuclein in Parkinson's, huntingtin in Huntington's) → cancer (loss of autophagy → genomic instability → transformation; but established tumors may use autophagy for survival under nutrient stress) → accelerated aging → susceptibility to infections. (5) Therapeutic potential: rapamycin (mTOR inhibitor) enhances autophagy → extends lifespan in model organisms; autophagy modulation being explored for cancer, neurodegeneration, and anti-aging.
